The Effect of Coercion on Contracts: Fasid or Mawquf?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of coercion on contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the effect of coercion on contracts, explain the evidence for each, and apply the relied-upon view (Zufar) to determine the legal status of a coerced contract.

Core Idea
Coercion renders a contract suspended, not void; ownership doesn't transfer without ratification.

The passage presents a critical refinement within the Hanafi school: should coercion invalidate a contract or merely suspend it? This distinction has profound practical consequences for ownership and rescission. We will analyse the arguments of Abu Hanifa and Zufar and identify the mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State clearly that Abu Hanifa considers a coerced contract as fasid (void) while Zufar considers it mawquf (suspended).
Both are Hanafi imams.
2
Understand the legal category of each
Fasid means the contract is defective but may transfer ownership upon execution, with a right of rescission. Mawquf means the contract is valid but suspended, producing no effect until ratification.
The difference hinges on whether the defect is in the essence or in the enforceability.
3
Analyse Zufar's evidence from ratification
Zufar argues that a coerced contract can be ratified after coercion ends, which is a characteristic of suspended contracts, not void ones. A fasid contract cannot be cured by ratification.
This is the key proof for suspension.
4
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, ownership transfers upon execution (e.g., delivery of sold item) and the coerced party can only rescind. Under Zufar, ownership does not transfer until ratification; if no ratification, the contract remains ineffective.
This affects who bears risk and how ownership disputes are resolved.
5
Identify the tarjih and mufta bihi
The author sides with Zufar, stating his view is 'awjah' (more correct) and notes that the Majalla (Article 1006) adopted it. Thus, the relied-upon view is that a coerced contract is mawquf.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
قال ابن المنذر لا خلاف لأحد من أهل العلم في عدم جواز هذا الاشتراط» (ر: فتح القدير ٤٠٢/٥).
وفي المزارعة والمساقاة : إذا اشترط كذلك لأحد الطرفين قدر معين مقطوع من محصول الأرض أو ثمر الشجر فسدت المزارعة للغرر. فيجب لصحة العقد أن تكون الحصص من الحاصل شائعة نسبية .
ثالثاً : الإكراه
٥/٥٥ - وأما الإكراه فقد اختلف الرأي في الاجتهاد الحنفي حول أثره في العقود إذا عقدت بالإكراه : هل يفسدها إفساداً، أو يجعلها موقوفة؟ فذهب الإمام أبو حنيفة إلى أن الإكراه يفسد العقد إفساداً، وتترتب عليه سائر أحكام العقد الفاسد. حتى إنه لو كان الإكراه في بيع أو هبة مثلاً تنتقل فيه ملكية المبيع أو الموهوب إذا تم تنفيذ العقد بالتسليم، وفقاً للأحكام العامة في العقد الفاسد، كما سنرى في نتائج الفساد.
- وذهب زُفَرُ بن الهُذيل من كبار أصحاب أبي حنيفة إلى أن العقد مع الإكراه صحيح موقوف لا فاسد فلا يفيد حكمه بالتنفيذ، بدليل أنه يقبل الإجازة بعد زوال الإكراه باتفاق الآراء. وهذا من دلائل التوقف لا الفساد فإن الفاسد يفسخ فسخاً ولا يرتفع فساده بالإجازة. فالإكراه مانع للنفاذ لا للصحة .
وقد تقدم إيضاح ذلك في مبحث شوائب الإرادة من نظرية العقود فلينظر (ف٣/٣٤ متناً وحاشية).
فعلى رأي من رأى الإكراه مفسداً للعقد إفساداً يكون الإكراه من أسباب الفساد العامة وعلى الرأي الآخر يخرج الإكراه عن هذا الموضوع إذ يكون من موانع النفاذ.
وقد بينا هناك أن رأي زفر أوجه من رأي الإمام فيما يظهر، وأن المادة / ١٠٠٦/ من المجلة يشعر ظاهرها بأنها قد اختارت رأي زفر، إذ صرحت بأن العقود الواقعة بالإكراه غير معتبرة، ولكن إذا أجازها العاقد المستكره بعد زوال الإكراه عنه «تعتبر". فعدم الاعتبار مفاده عدم ثبوت حكم العقد ولو تم تنفيذه. وهذا إنما يتمشى على رأي زفر بالتوقف، لأنَّ العقد الموقوف هو الذي لا يفيد حكمه قبل الإجازة، ولا تنتقل فيه الملكية إذا كان ناقلاً لها ولو تم تنفيذه. أما أبو حنيفة فيقول بانتقال الملكية في العقد المعقود بالإكراه إذا تم تنفيذه ككل عقد فاسد وإنما للمستكره حق الفسخ كما أسلفنا بيانه.
٦/٥٥ - هذه هي الأسباب العامة الثلاثة للفساد في الاجتهاد الحنفي. ويليها في العموم الشرط المفسد، وهو الشرط التقييدي الممنوع الذي تقدم بيانه في بحث الشروط العقدية من نظرية العقود (ف ١٤/٤٣) فإن الشرط المفسد يفسد جميع عقود المعاوضات كالبيع والاستصناع والإجارة والصلح والقسمة والمخارجة، وكذا عقود الشركة والمزارعة والمساقاة.
وما سوى ذلك فأسباب خاصة ينحصر تأثيرها في بعض العقود. فالشيوع مثلاً يفسد الرهن والتوقيت يفسد البيع، وعدم التوقيت يفسد الإجارة، وعدم التقابض في مجلس العقد يفسد عقد الصرف... إلى غير ذلك مما يرجع في معرفته إلى شرائط الصحة في كل عقد على حدة.
Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Grounds in Fiqh. Ibn al-Mundhir said: 'There is no disagreement among any of the scholars regarding the impermissibility of this condition' (see: Fath al-Qadir 5/402). And in sharecropping (muzara'ah) and musaqah: if a fixed amount from the land's produce or tree's fruit is stipulated for one of the parties, the sharecropping becomes invalid due to uncertainty (gharar). For the validity of the contract, the shares must be proportionate and undivided. Third: Coercion. 5/55 - As for coercion, there is a difference of opinion in Hanafi ijtihad regarding its effect on contracts made under coercion: does it render them void (fasid), or make them suspended (mawquf)? Imam Abu Hanifa held that coercion renders the contract void, and the rest of the rulings of a void contract apply. Thus, if in a sale or gift, the ownership of the sold item or gifted property is transferred if the contract is executed by delivery, according to the general rules of a void contract, as we will see in the consequences of invalidity. - And Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, one of the senior companions of Abu Hanifa, held that a contract made under coercion is valid but suspended, not void, so it does not produce its legal effect upon execution, based on the evidence that it can be ratified after the coercion ceases, by unanimous agreement. This is evidence of suspension, not invalidity, for a void contract is rescinded and its invalidity is not removed by ratification. Thus, coercion is an impediment to enforceability, not to validity. This has been explained previously in the section on defects of will in the theory of contracts, so refer to it (volume 3, page 34, text and commentary). According to the view that sees coercion as invalidating the contract, coercion is among the general causes of invalidity; on the other view, coercion exits this topic and becomes an impediment to enforceability. And we have shown there that Zufar's view is more correct than the Imam's view in what appears, and that Article 1006 of the Majalla apparently adopted Zufar's view, as it stated that contracts made under coercion are not considered, but if the coerced party ratifies them after the coercion ceases, 'they are considered.' So 'not considered' means that the contract's ruling is not established even if executed. This is consistent with Zufar's view of suspension, because a suspended contract does not produce its ruling before ratification, and ownership does not transfer even if executed. As for Abu Hanifa, he says that ownership transfers in a contract made under coercion if it is executed, like any void contract, and the coerced party only has the right of rescission, as we have previously explained. 6/55 - These are the three general causes of invalidity in Hanafi ijtihad. And following them in generality is the prohibited condition, which is the restrictive prohibited condition that was explained in the discussion of contractual conditions in the theory of contracts (section 14/43). For the invalidating condition corrupts all commutative contracts such as sale, istisna', lease, compromise, division, and mukharaja, as well as contracts of partnership, sharecropping, and musaqah. Other than that are specific causes whose effect is limited to certain contracts. For example, co-ownership invalidates a pledge, time limitation invalidates a sale, lack of time limitation invalidates a lease, and lack of hand-to-hand exchange in the contract session invalidates a currency exchange contract... and so on, knowledge of which refers to the conditions of validity for each contract individually.
Source pages p. 704

Commentary
This passage is a concluding section of a discussion on the general causes of contract invalidity (fasad) in Hanafi fiqh. It begins with an example of a prohibited condition in sharecropping (muzara'ah) and musaqah: stipulating a fixed quantity of produce for one party renders the contract invalid due to uncertainty (gharar). The shares must be proportionate and undivided (shai'ah nisbiyyah). Then it proceeds to the third general cause: coercion (ikrah). The author presents the ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl. Abu Hanifa considers coercion a cause that renders the contract void (fasid), meaning that ownership transfers upon execution (e.g., in a sale or gift) and the coerced party only has the right of rescission. Zufar, on the other hand, holds that the contract is valid but suspended (mawquf), meaning it produces no legal effect until ratified after the coercion ends. The evidence for Zufar's position is that the contract can be ratified after coercion ceases, which is a characteristic of suspended contracts, not void ones. The author notes that Zufar's view appears stronger (awjah) and that Article 1006 of the Majalla (Ottoman Civil Code) apparently adopted it, stating that contracts made under coercion are 'not considered' but become considered upon ratification. This means ownership does not transfer even if executed, unlike Abu Hanifa's position. The passage then summarizes that these are the three general causes of invalidity in Hanafi ijtihad (though only coercion is discussed here; the other two are presumably from earlier pages). It adds that the prohibited condition (al-shart al-mufsid) is also a general cause that invalidates all commutative contracts (e.g., sale, lease, partnership, sharecropping). Finally, it mentions specific causes that only affect particular contracts, such as co-ownership invalidating a pledge, time limitation invalidating a sale, lack of time limitation invalidating a lease, and lack of hand-to-hand exchange (qabd) in the contract session invalidating a currency exchange contract. The passage serves as a methodological guide for a mufti to understand which contracts are affected by general versus specific causes of invalidity.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Coercion that compels a person to enter a contract against their will, rendering the contract either fasid (Abu Hanifa) or mawquf (Zufar).
العقد الفاسد (al-'aqd al-fasid): A contract that is defective in its conditions but valid in its essence; it is not wholly void but can be rescinded, and it may transfer ownership upon execution.
العقد الموقوف (al-'aqd al-mawquf): A contract that is sound (sahih) but suspended, producing no legal effect until ratified by the person whose consent was defective (e.g., due to coercion).
إجازة (ijazah): Ratification or approval given after the contract is made, which validates a suspended contract retroactively.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (as per the Majalla and the author), a contract made under coercion is:
A) fasid
B) mawquf
C) batil
D) sahih

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that coercion suspends a contract until ratification; it does not invalidate it.

The Prohibited Condition (al-Shart al-Mufsid) as a General Cause of Contract Invalidity
Explanation of the prohibited condition as a general cause of contract invalidity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a prohibited condition and identify which contracts are affected by it according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A condition that contradicts the nature of a commutative contract invalidates it — the shart mufsid is a general cause of fasad.

After enumerating the three general causes of invalidity, the author introduces the prohibited condition as an additional general cause. This lesson clarifies what constitutes a prohibited condition and its sweeping effect across all commutative contracts.

Guided Path
1
Define shart mufsid
It is a restrictive condition that is prohibited (mamnu') and invalidates the contract. It was discussed earlier in the theory of contracts.
Not all conditions are invalidating; only those that are restrictive and contradict the contract's nature.
2
Identify which contracts it affects
The passage states that it invalidates all commutative contracts (e.g., sale, lease, istisna', compromise, division, mukharaja) and also contracts of partnership, sharecropping, and musaqah.
Non-commutative contracts like gift or loan are not mentioned; they may have different rules.
3
Distinguish from specific causes
Specific causes affect only certain contracts (e.g., co-ownership invalidates a pledge; time limitation invalidates a sale). The shart mufsid is general.
This helps a mufti classify whether a problem is general or specific.
4
Apply to examples from the text
In muzara'ah, stipulating a fixed quantity of produce is a prohibited condition because it introduces uncertainty (gharar) and violates the proportionate sharing required.
The shares must be 'shai'ah nisbiyyah' (proportionate and undivided).
5
Note the legal consequence
The contract becomes fasid (defective). It may still have some effect (e.g., transfer of ownership if executed) but can be rescinded.
This aligns with the general category of fasid contracts.

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
قال ابن المنذر لا خلاف لأحد من أهل العلم في عدم جواز هذا الاشتراط» (ر: فتح القدير ٤٠٢/٥).
وفي المزارعة والمساقاة : إذا اشترط كذلك لأحد الطرفين قدر معين مقطوع من محصول الأرض أو ثمر الشجر فسدت المزارعة للغرر. فيجب لصحة العقد أن تكون الحصص من الحاصل شائعة نسبية .
ثالثاً : الإكراه
٥/٥٥ - وأما الإكراه فقد اختلف الرأي في الاجتهاد الحنفي حول أثره في العقود إذا عقدت بالإكراه : هل يفسدها إفساداً، أو يجعلها موقوفة؟ فذهب الإمام أبو حنيفة إلى أن الإكراه يفسد العقد إفساداً، وتترتب عليه سائر أحكام العقد الفاسد. حتى إنه لو كان الإكراه في بيع أو هبة مثلاً تنتقل فيه ملكية المبيع أو الموهوب إذا تم تنفيذ العقد بالتسليم، وفقاً للأحكام العامة في العقد الفاسد، كما سنرى في نتائج الفساد.
- وذهب زُفَرُ بن الهُذيل من كبار أصحاب أبي حنيفة إلى أن العقد مع الإكراه صحيح موقوف لا فاسد فلا يفيد حكمه بالتنفيذ، بدليل أنه يقبل الإجازة بعد زوال الإكراه باتفاق الآراء. وهذا من دلائل التوقف لا الفساد فإن الفاسد يفسخ فسخاً ولا يرتفع فساده بالإجازة. فالإكراه مانع للنفاذ لا للصحة .
وقد تقدم إيضاح ذلك في مبحث شوائب الإرادة من نظرية العقود فلينظر (ف٣/٣٤ متناً وحاشية).
فعلى رأي من رأى الإكراه مفسداً للعقد إفساداً يكون الإكراه من أسباب الفساد العامة وعلى الرأي الآخر يخرج الإكراه عن هذا الموضوع إذ يكون من موانع النفاذ.
وقد بينا هناك أن رأي زفر أوجه من رأي الإمام فيما يظهر، وأن المادة / ١٠٠٦/ من المجلة يشعر ظاهرها بأنها قد اختارت رأي زفر، إذ صرحت بأن العقود الواقعة بالإكراه غير معتبرة، ولكن إذا أجازها العاقد المستكره بعد زوال الإكراه عنه «تعتبر". فعدم الاعتبار مفاده عدم ثبوت حكم العقد ولو تم تنفيذه. وهذا إنما يتمشى على رأي زفر بالتوقف، لأنَّ العقد الموقوف هو الذي لا يفيد حكمه قبل الإجازة، ولا تنتقل فيه الملكية إذا كان ناقلاً لها ولو تم تنفيذه. أما أبو حنيفة فيقول بانتقال الملكية في العقد المعقود بالإكراه إذا تم تنفيذه ككل عقد فاسد وإنما للمستكره حق الفسخ كما أسلفنا بيانه.
٦/٥٥ - هذه هي الأسباب العامة الثلاثة للفساد في الاجتهاد الحنفي. ويليها في العموم الشرط المفسد، وهو الشرط التقييدي الممنوع الذي تقدم بيانه في بحث الشروط العقدية من نظرية العقود (ف ١٤/٤٣) فإن الشرط المفسد يفسد جميع عقود المعاوضات كالبيع والاستصناع والإجارة والصلح والقسمة والمخارجة، وكذا عقود الشركة والمزارعة والمساقاة.
وما سوى ذلك فأسباب خاصة ينحصر تأثيرها في بعض العقود. فالشيوع مثلاً يفسد الرهن والتوقيت يفسد البيع، وعدم التوقيت يفسد الإجارة، وعدم التقابض في مجلس العقد يفسد عقد الصرف... إلى غير ذلك مما يرجع في معرفته إلى شرائط الصحة في كل عقد على حدة.
Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Grounds in Fiqh. Ibn al-Mundhir said: 'There is no disagreement among any of the scholars regarding the impermissibility of this condition' (see: Fath al-Qadir 5/402). And in sharecropping (muzara'ah) and musaqah: if a fixed amount from the land's produce or tree's fruit is stipulated for one of the parties, the sharecropping becomes invalid due to uncertainty (gharar). For the validity of the contract, the shares must be proportionate and undivided. Third: Coercion. 5/55 - As for coercion, there is a difference of opinion in Hanafi ijtihad regarding its effect on contracts made under coercion: does it render them void (fasid), or make them suspended (mawquf)? Imam Abu Hanifa held that coercion renders the contract void, and the rest of the rulings of a void contract apply. Thus, if in a sale or gift, the ownership of the sold item or gifted property is transferred if the contract is executed by delivery, according to the general rules of a void contract, as we will see in the consequences of invalidity. - And Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, one of the senior companions of Abu Hanifa, held that a contract made under coercion is valid but suspended, not void, so it does not produce its legal effect upon execution, based on the evidence that it can be ratified after the coercion ceases, by unanimous agreement. This is evidence of suspension, not invalidity, for a void contract is rescinded and its invalidity is not removed by ratification. Thus, coercion is an impediment to enforceability, not to validity. This has been explained previously in the section on defects of will in the theory of contracts, so refer to it (volume 3, page 34, text and commentary). According to the view that sees coercion as invalidating the contract, coercion is among the general causes of invalidity; on the other view, coercion exits this topic and becomes an impediment to enforceability. And we have shown there that Zufar's view is more correct than the Imam's view in what appears, and that Article 1006 of the Majalla apparently adopted Zufar's view, as it stated that contracts made under coercion are not considered, but if the coerced party ratifies them after the coercion ceases, 'they are considered.' So 'not considered' means that the contract's ruling is not established even if executed. This is consistent with Zufar's view of suspension, because a suspended contract does not produce its ruling before ratification, and ownership does not transfer even if executed. As for Abu Hanifa, he says that ownership transfers in a contract made under coercion if it is executed, like any void contract, and the coerced party only has the right of rescission, as we have previously explained. 6/55 - These are the three general causes of invalidity in Hanafi ijtihad. And following them in generality is the prohibited condition, which is the restrictive prohibited condition that was explained in the discussion of contractual conditions in the theory of contracts (section 14/43). For the invalidating condition corrupts all commutative contracts such as sale, istisna', lease, compromise, division, and mukharaja, as well as contracts of partnership, sharecropping, and musaqah. Other than that are specific causes whose effect is limited to certain contracts. For example, co-ownership invalidates a pledge, time limitation invalidates a sale, lack of time limitation invalidates a lease, and lack of hand-to-hand exchange in the contract session invalidates a currency exchange contract... and so on, knowledge of which refers to the conditions of validity for each contract individually.

Key Terms
الشرط المفسد (al-shart al-mufsid): A prohibited restrictive condition that invalidates the contract; it is a general cause of fasad affecting all commutative contracts.
الغرر (gharar): Uncertainty or risk in a contract that can lead to invalidity; in muzara'ah, stipulating a fixed quantity creates gharar.

Key Takeaway

Rescinding a Fasid Contract: Conditions, Rights, and the Purifying Qawida
Explains two conditions for rescinding a fasid contract, who may rescind, and the rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two conditions for rescinding a fasid contract, identify who holds the right to rescind per Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, and use the qawida 'disposal purifies ownership' in fatwa reasoning.

Core Idea
Rescission of a fasid contract is barred if the subject matter changes or if third-party rights vest.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi framework for rescinding an invalid (fasid) contract. The school imposes two strict conditions to protect transactional stability and acquired rights. We also explore an intra-Hanafi disagreement on who may exercise the rescission right, and uncover the powerful qawida that disposal purifies ownership—a solution to the problem of unpublicized transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions for rescission
From the passage, list the two conditions that must be satisfied for rescission to be possible: (1) the subject matter remains unchanged after execution, and (2) rescission does not invalidate third-party rights.
Both conditions aim to protect stability and acquired rights.
2
Understand why changes block rescission
Analyze the examples: if the buyer grinds wheat, builds on land, or sells to a third party, the subject matter is altered or rights have vested. Rescission is prevented, and ownership becomes stable.
The key is that the contract had some effect (ownership by possession) that cannot be undone without harm.
3
Compare Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad on who may rescind
Abu Hanifa gives both parties (and the judge) the right to rescind regardless of the cause. Imam Muhammad restricts this: if the fasād is due to a condition benefiting one party, only that party may rescind.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
4
Relate the rulings to the qawida that disposal purifies ownership
The passage ends with a qawida: disposal of an asset purifies it from defects in ownership. This explains why third-party rights are protected—if B sells to C, C's ownership is clean and A cannot reclaim.
This qawida stabilizes transactions despite the absence of formal publicity.
5
Apply these principles in a fatwa context
As a mufti, use the muftā bihī position (Abu Hanifa's view is generally relied upon) and the qawida to resolve disputes over fasid sales, especially where goods have been resold or transformed.
Always consider the conditions for rescission; if either is absent, the contract is stabilized.

Chapter (56): Result of Invalidity and Related Matters
Second: Entitlement to Rescission and Its Impediments:
56/5 - As for entitlement to rescission due to invalidity, it is restricted by two conditions:
First: The subject matter remains in its original state after execution of the contract.
Rescission of an invalid sale is prevented if the sold item perishes with the buyer, or he consumes it, or changes its form in a way that alters its name, such as if it was wheat and he grinds it, or was flour and he bakes it, and likewise if he adds an increase, such as if the sold item was a house and he builds it, or land and he plants trees in it, or a garment and he dyes it.
And the prevention of rescission in all these cases and their likes results in the stability of ownership or the ruling that was established by execution of the invalid contract.
Second: That rescission of the invalid contract does not lead to invalidating rights that have been acquired by non-contracting parties in the subject matter; otherwise the right arising for a third party is a legal impediment to rescinding the previous contract due to invalidity.
On this basis, if the buyer takes possession of the sold item in an invalid sale so that he is considered owner thereof by possession, then he sells it, gives it as a gift, mortgages it with a valid contract, or endows it, rescission of the first invalid sale is prevented and the ruling established by its execution becomes stable (cf. Majallah / 372)
(1)
And al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar on rulings of invalid sale 4/125-127 and 130-131, and al-Bada'i' 5/300-302.
And the goal of the jurists in preventing rescission despite invalidity when these two conditions are absent is only to ensure stability of dealings and protection of acquired rights.
6/5 - To whom does the authority to rescind belong in an invalid contract?
And the authority to rescind is established for each of the two contracting parties, provided that it is in the presence of the other, regardless of the cause of invalidity according to Abu Hanifa, and likewise the judge has this authority (cf. 1/56 and al-Bada'i' 5/301).
But his companion Imam Muhammad held that if the invalidity arises from a corrupt condition stipulated for the benefit of one of the parties over the other, the right of rescission is restricted to the party for whose benefit the corrupt condition was stipulated, because he is able to correct the contract by deleting the condition; if the other were allowed to rescind, that would nullify his right to correct and confirm the contract (cf. al-Bada'i' 5/300).
7/56 - Professor Shafiq Shihata says in his study of invalidity, in summary: The statement that ownership transfers to the buyer by possession in an invalid sale is a purely Hanafi conceptualization, and it is the essence of the theory of invalidity; its actual purpose is to protect third-party rights, so it is not valid to argue the invalidity of the contract against this third party. And the owner of something that left his ownership by an invalid contract cannot reclaim it if it has passed through hands, because if that were allowed for him, contractual relations would be destabilized.
The causes of invalidity are many, and there are no methods in Islamic jurisprudence for publicizing transactions (1), so the third party remains ignorant of defects in the ownership of the person from whom he acquired ownership. If he buys, his ownership is shaky. For this reason, the jurists devised a highly beneficial rule by which they could dispense with methods of publicity: that they considered disposal of the asset
·
purifies it from the defects that taint the owner's ownership.
But how is it possible for a person to give ownership of more than he owns?
It appears that this problem led the jurists to conceptualize the transfer of ownership to
(1) I say: There are also no methods in modern Western jurisprudence for publicizing transactions involving movable assets such that anyone wishing can know of them; rather, that is impossible for movables. Therefore, legal methods of publicity nowadays are limited to real estate transactions by recording them in the property sheet of the land registry, because it is possible for each property to have a register, unlike movables.
754
Source pages p. 710

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi explanation of the conditions for rescinding a fasid (invalid) contract, the impediments to rescission, and who holds the power to rescind. The author first states two conditions that must be met for rescission to be possible: (1) the subject matter must remain unchanged after contract execution, and (2) rescission must not invalidate rights acquired by third parties in the subject matter. If either condition is absent, rescission is barred and the ruling (e.g., ownership) becomes stable. The Hanafi school gives both contracting parties (and the judge) the right to rescind regardless of the cause of invalidity, but Imam Muhammad restricts this: if the invalidity is due to a condition benefiting one party, only that party may rescind because he can fix the contract by dropping the condition. The passage ends with a scholarly commentary by Shafiq Shihata explaining the Hanafi theory of fasid as a means to protect third-party rights and stabilize transactions, despite the absence of formal publicity methods. All examples are from Hanafi fiqh, such as wheat being ground or a house being rebuilt, illustrating that changes prevent rescission.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract, returning the parties to their pre-contract state.
فساد (fasād): Invalidity in a contract due to a defect, such as a corrupt condition, which prevents the contract from being fully sound but may still produce some legal effects (like ownership by possession).
قبض (qabd): Possession of the subject matter; in a fasid sale, possession transfers ownership to the buyer, making him liable for the price.
قاعدة التصرف يطهر العين (qā‘idat al-taṣarruf yuṭahhir al-‘ayn): The legal maxim that disposal of an asset purifies it from defects in the owner's title, protecting good-faith third parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does rescission of a fasid sale become impossible?
A) When the buyer grinds the wheat into flour.
B) When the buyer pays the price.
C) When the seller cancels the contract.
D) When the contract is not yet executed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school bars rescission of a fasid contract to protect stability and third-party rights; disposal of the subject matter purifies it from defects.

The Fetal Stage of Legal Capacity: Suspended Ownership and the Dhimma
Defines the fetal stage and its incomplete legal capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fetal ownership and dhimma, apply the concepts of suspended ownership and estimated life to inheritance and diyat cases, and compare the Hanbali view.

Core Idea
Hanafi: fetus has no dhimma; ownership is suspended pending live birth – Hanbali differs.

Islamic law considers the fetus to be in a state of incomplete legal capacity (ahliyyah). This lesson explores the Hanafi view that the fetus lacks a dhimma – the notional vessel for obligations – so its property rights are contingent upon live birth. We will also examine the Hanbali counter-position and the special case of the ghurrah (blood money for a miscarried fetus). Understanding these principles is essential for a mufti when issuing fatwas on inheritance, bequests, and diyat involving a fetus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stage and its legal capacity
Recognise that the fetal stage (tawr al-ijtinān) is the first stage of legal capacity. The passage states that the fetus is not yet a legal person for obligations (lā dhimma lahu). This means it cannot be burdened with debts or contractual duties.
The dhimma is an attribute of a living person; the fetus is not yet fully a person.
2
Understand suspended ownership
Any property rights reserved for the fetus (inheritance, will, endowment income) are not immediately owned. They are milkiyyah mutaʿalliqah – suspended ownership – which only becomes effective if the fetus is born alive. If born dead, the rights revert to others.
Suspended ownership is a Hanafi technical term distinguishing from immediate ownership.
3
Compare Hanbali view
The passage notes that Hanbali jurisprudence attributes a dhimma to the fetus and considers its ownership effective from the time of the cause, e.g., the testator's death. This leads to practical differences in who receives maintenance from the fetus's inheritance.
The Hanbali view is transmitted from Imam Ahmad.
4
Apply the ghurrah exception
If a fetus is miscarried due to criminal assault, it is legally considered to have been alive (ḥayāh taqdīriyyah) for the purposes of diyah and inheritance. The assailant must pay the ghurrah (500 dirhams), and the fetus's share of inheritance goes to its heirs.
This is an exception to the general rule that the fetus must be born alive to own.

Chapter (60): The First Stage of Capacity: The Stage of Concealment (Fetal Stage). Necessity, and because purchase would obligate him to pay the price, and he is not qualified for obligation (1). 60.5 - It is noted that what is reserved for the fetus from inheritance, will, or endowment income does not constitute effective ownership for the fetus, but is suspended upon his being born alive (2). If he is born dead, nothing of those suspended rights is established; the reserved endowment income reverts to other beneficiaries, the inheritance reserved for him reverts to the other heirs of his testator, and what was bequeathed to him reverts to the testator's heirs. If the fetus is born alive, even by estimation (3), his ownership in these rights is established, retroactively based on the time of their prior cause. (1) Hence our jurists state that the fetus has no dhimma (legal personality for obligations) (see: Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi 4/237), because the dhimma is a notional vessel for the stability of rights that are upon a person, as we previously indicated (see vol. 59, no. 10); so there is no need to assume it for him before he is qualified for obligations. Further clarification will come in the discussion of the theory of dhimma from the part of this jurisprudential series. (2) In Hanbali jurisprudence, the fetus's ownership is considered effective in inheritance from the time of the testator's death. They derived that a pregnant woman and her fetus's relatives entitled to maintenance are supported from the fetus's inheritance and other wealth if he has property; this is transmitted from Imam Ahmad himself (see: al-Qawa'id li Ibn Rajab al-Hanbali under rule 84, p. 181, first edition, al-Sidq Printing Press, Cairo). This also indicates that in Hanbali jurisprudence, the fetus has capacity for obligation and a dhimma. (3) The fetus's life is considered estimable if it is miscarried due to an assault on its mother, such as if someone strikes a pregnant woman and she miscarries a dead fetus; then this fetus is legally considered alive and that he died by the act of the assailant. The assailant is obligated to pay the blood money for the fetus, called al-Ghurrah, amounting to five hundred dirhams equaling fifty dinars, each ten dirhams per dinar, given to the fetus's heirs. Likewise, the fetus's right in inheritance from its testator who died earlier is established; the fetus's share of the inheritance is given to its own heirs and is not returned to the heirs of its testator (see: al-Durr al-Mukhtar, chapter on the fetus from the book of diyat 5/377-378). The weight of a dinar is a mithqal of gold (the weight of a mithqal today is 4.25 grams). This standard is considered in all financial obligations, religious and civil (see: Radd al-Muhtar, section on zakat of wealth 2/29).
Source pages p. 747

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the fetal stage of legal capacity (ahliyyah). It opens by stating that the fetus lacks a dhimma—the notional container for obligations—so it cannot be held liable for contracts. The key rule is that any property rights reserved for the fetus (inheritance, will, endowment proceeds) are not immediately vested; they are suspended (muta'alliqah) pending live birth. If born dead, nothing is established; the rights revert to other rightful parties. If born alive, ownership is retroactive to the original cause (e.g., the time of the testator's death). The passage then contrasts the Hanafi position with the Hanbali school, which attributes a dhimma to the fetus and immediate ownership. Hanafi proofs are based on the absence of dhimma and the fetus's inability to undertake obligations. The passage also discusses the estimated life of a fetus (الغرة) in cases of criminal miscarriage: if a fetus is miscarried dead due to assault, it is considered alive for purposes of diyah (blood money) and inheritance rights. The diyah for a fetus is five hundred dirhams or fifty dinars. This third note explains that even a stillbirth from violence is legally treated as a living person who died from the act.

Key Terms
الذمة (al-dhimmah): A notional vessel (wi'ā' iʿtibārī) for the stability of rights and obligations that are upon a person, absent in the fetus.
ملكية متوقفة (milkiyyah mutaʿalliqah): Ownership that is suspended pending a condition—here, live birth. Not effective until the condition occurs.
غرة (ghurrah): The blood money (diyah) for a fetus, fixed at five hundred dirhams (fifty dinars), paid when the fetus is miscarried dead due to assault.
أهلية الالتزام (ahliyyat al-iltizām): Capacity to be bound by obligations; the fetus lacks this in Hanafi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From the moment of the testator's death
B) At the moment of birth, but only if the fetus is born alive
C) Only after the fetus reaches puberty
D) At the moment of conception

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the fetus has no dhimma and its property rights are suspended until live birth, with a single exception for criminal miscarriage where the fetus is legally considered alive.

The Non-Discerning Child: No Capacity for Performance
Defining childhood stage and its legal consequences for capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tamyiz as understood in Hanafi fiqh and state the ruling that a non-discerning child's constructive acts are void.

Core Idea
A child without tamyiz has no capacity for performance; all his constructive acts are void.

This passage defines the childhood stage before discernment (tamyiz). It explains that while a child's capacity for obligations (ahliyyat al-wujub) expands at birth, he still lacks capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada') until he attains tamyiz. Consequently, any contracts or gifts he makes are legally void.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of legal capacity
Recall that ahliyyah is divided into ahliyyat al-wujub (capacity for rights) and ahliyyat al-ada' (capacity for performance). The non-discerning child has the first but not the second.
Ahliyyat al-ada' depends on reason and understanding.
2
Define tamyiz
Tamyiz is the ability to understand legislative address (khitab tashri'i) and basic transactional consequences. The child must grasp the difference between sale and purchase and understand value.
Tamyiz is not merely natural discernment but legal understanding.
3
Identify the non-discerning child (ghayr mumayyiz)
A child who has not attained tamyiz is called ghayr mumayyiz. He may have perception in natural matters but lacks legal comprehension.
The threshold is cognitive, not strictly age-based.
4
Apply the ruling on constructive acts
Because the child lacks ahliyyat al-ada', any constructive disposition (e.g., sale, gift, divorce) he performs is void (batil) and given no legal effect.
This applies to both statements and actions.

Source Text

فالطفل في طور طفولته قبل أن يصل إلى هذا الوعي والتمييز يعتبر ويسمى : غير مميز وإن كان له إدراك وتمييز في كثير من الأمور الطبيعية . ٢/٦١ - إن مما لا ريب فيه أنَّ خروج الجنين إلى الدنيا بالولادة ينتقل فيه الإنسان من طَوْر إلى طور أساسيين في حياته وقابلياته : فهو ينتقل من طور هو فيه كجزء تابع لأمه في الحياة إلى طور هو فيه ذو وجود مستقل وقابليات طبيعية لم تكن له من قبل.

/٣/٦١ - ولفقدان أهلية الأداء في الطفل غير المميز لا يعتد بشيء من التصرفات الإنشائية يباشره بنفسه من أقوال أو أفعال.
Chapter 61: The Second Stage of Capacity: The Stage of Childhood. 1/61 - This stage in a human extends from the time his mother gives birth to him until he becomes discerning (mumayyiz). What is meant by his discernment (tamyiz) is that he attains consciousness and perception by which he understands legislative address in general—he grasps the meanings of religious acts and civil transactions, and understands the results of these transactions in the exchange of rights and obligations, even in a simple, summary manner. He knows the difference between sale and purchase, and understands the equality and inequality of values. So the child in his childhood stage before reaching this consciousness and discernment is considered and called non-discerning (ghayr mumayyiz), even if he has perception and discernment in many natural matters. 2/61 - There is no doubt that the exit of the fetus into the world at birth transfers the human from one stage to another fundamental stage in his life and capacities: he moves from a stage where he was like a part dependent on his mother in life to a stage where he has an independent existence and natural capacities he did not have before. Accordingly, his legal capacity must expand and enter a more complete phase. However, it can only expand within the scope of capacity for obligations (ahliyyat al-wujub) and cannot extend to the sphere of capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada'), because consciousness is absent in him. The child remains lacking, in this stage, the capacity for performance, because capacity for performance only qualifies him for religious or civil acts that depend on reason, and the child does not comprehend these acts at all at the beginning of this stage, nor does he understand them sufficiently at its end. 3/61 - Due to the lack of capacity for performance in the non-discerning child, no consideration is given to any constructive (insha'i) disposition he performs himself, whether statements or actions.
Source pages p. 748

Commentary
This passage is a ruling defining the childhood stage of legal capacity (ahliyyah) before the child attains discernment (tamyiz). It first defines tamyiz as the ability to understand legislative discourse and basic transactional concepts (e.g., knowing the difference between sale and purchase, understanding value). Until that point, the child is non-discerning (ghayr mumayyiz). The passage then explains the transition from fetus to born child: the child becomes an independent being, so his capacity for obligations (ahliyyat al-wujub) expands, but he still lacks capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada') due to absence of consciousness and reason. Therefore, any constructive acts (such as contracts or gifts) performed by a non-discerning child are void and given no legal effect. This is a fundamental rule in Hanafi fiqh: a child without tamyiz cannot perform valid transactions; even after tamyiz but before puberty, transactions require guardian approval.

Key Terms
التمييز (al-tamyīz): The ability to understand legislative address and basic legal consequences of transactions, distinguishing between beneficial and harmful acts.
غير مميز (ghayr mumayyiz): A child who lacks tamyiz; he cannot understand legislative discourse or legal consequences of acts, despite having natural discernment.
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-adāʾ): Capacity for performance—the legal competence to perform religious and civil acts that depend on reason and understanding.
تصرفات إنشائية (taṣarrufāt inshāʾiyyah): Constructive dispositions, such as contracts, gifts, divorces, etc., that create new legal effects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Valid only with guardian approval
C) Void (bāṭil) and given no legal effect
D) Valid but voidable if disadvantageous

Key Takeaway
The non-discerning child lacks capacity for performance; any constructive act he performs is void.

The Discerning Minor's Harmful Acts and the Judge's Loan Exception
States voidness of minor's divorce and exception for judge's loan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ruling on a discerning minor's harmful transactions and apply the exception for a judge lending orphan property, using the Hanafi framework of legal capacity.

Core Idea
A discerning minor's pure harm acts are void; a judge may lend orphan property to guarantee its safety.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam illustrates a key rasm al-mufti principle: the general rule that a discerning minor's pure harm transactions are void, with a specific exception for the judge's lending of orphan property. Understanding this rule and exception is crucial for a mufti when advising on guardianship and property management for minors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal capacity stage
Recognize that the passage refers to the third stage of legal capacity (ahliyyah) – the stage of discernment (tamyiz). A discerning minor (ṣaghīr mumayyiz) has deficient capacity for harmful acts.
Tamyiz typically starts around age 7.
2
State the general ruling and its rationale
The divorce of a discerning minor is void (bāṭil) because it is pure harm (ḍarar maḥḍ) – the minor loses the mahr and marital benefits. Any transaction that is pure harm is not valid from a minor.
This is a general principle: minor's harmful acts are void.
3
Recognize the exception
An exception is made for a judge lending the orphan's property. Though lending has an element of donation, it is permitted because it protects the orphan's wealth from loss.
The exception exists because lending is guaranteed, unlike deposit.
4
Compare deposit vs loan in terms of guarantee
Deposit (wadīʿa) is an amāna (trust) – if lost without negligence, it is not guaranteed. Loan (qarḍ) is a ḍamān (guarantee) – the borrower must return the exact property or value in all circumstances.
Hence lending is safer for the orphan's property.
5
Apply the footnote to modern practice
The voidness of a minor's divorce does not prevent the wife from seeking judicial separation (tatlīq) under modern law. The right to sue depends on the plaintiff's capacity, not the defendant's.
This shows how classical rulings interface with contemporary legislation.

وقد استثنوا من هذا النوع إقراض القاضي مال اليتيم، وإن كان في القرض معنى التبرع ابتداءاً، فإنه يصون مال اليتيم من الضياع، وهو خير من الإيداع، لأن الوديعة إذا هلكت دون تعد من الوديع عليها أو تقصير في حفظها تهلك أمانة غير مضمونة بخلاف القرض فإنه مضمون على المقترض في جميع الأحوال وبسلطة القاضي إذا تولى هو الإقراض لا يخشى على مال القرض ( ر ) رد المحتار، الباب الأول من كتاب القضاء
.(٣٤١/٤
(1) الظاهر أن بطلان طلاق الصغير لا يمنع جواز دعوى طلب التفريق التي تقام من الزوجين على الآخر بمقتضى المادة / ۱۱۲ / من قانون الأحوال الشخصية الجديد لدينا، إذا ادعت الزوجة الكبيرة على زوجها الصغير بطلب التفريق، لأن هذا التفريق مخالعة جبرية أو تطليق جبري بسلطان القضاء عند عدم إمكان الإصلاح بين الزوجين. والقانون قد جعل طلب التفريق حقاً لكل من الزوجين، ولم يشرط في أحد منهما شرائط لصحة الادعاء به فيرجع في ذلك إلى القواعد العامة والقواعد تقضي بان ممارسة الحق تتوقف على أهلية صاحبه المدعي لا على أهلية الطرف الآخر المدعى عليه. فإذا كانت الزوجة كبيرة فالظاهر أن دعواها بالتفريق على زوجها الصغير القاصر وعة قانوناً وهذا كما لو كان الزوج مجنوناً، فإن جنونه لا يمنع زوجته من طلب التفريق بمقتضى أحكام القانون ومن المقرر شرعاً وقانوناً أن المجنون في حكم الصغير غير المميز كما صرحت به المادة /۹۷۹/ من المجلة، والمادة / ٤٧/ من القانون المدني الجديد الذي حل محلها.
مسموعة
Chapter 62: The third stage of legal capacity: the stage of discernment. His divorce is void because he loses the mahr and the benefit of marriage through it. They have made an exception from this type: the judge's lending of the orphan's money, even though lending has the meaning of donation initially, because it preserves the orphan's wealth from loss, and it is better than deposit, because if the deposit is lost without transgression or negligence on the part of the depositary, it is lost as an unguaranteed trust, unlike a loan which is guaranteed against the borrower in all circumstances, and with the judge's authority if he undertakes the lending, there is no fear for the loaned money. (Refer to Radd al-Muhtar, first chapter of the book of Judgments, 4/341). Footnote: It is apparent that the voidness of a minor's divorce does not prevent the permissibility of a lawsuit for separation filed by one spouse against the other under Article 112 of our new Personal Status Law, if the adult wife claims separation from her minor husband, because this separation is a compulsory khul' or a compulsory divorce by judicial authority when reconciliation between the spouses is not possible. The law has made the right to request separation a right for each spouse, and it did not stipulate in either of them conditions for the validity of the claim, so reference is made to the general rules, and the rules dictate that the exercise of a right depends on the capacity of its claimant, not on the capacity of the other party being sued. So if the wife is adult, it appears that her claim for separation against her minor husband is admissible legally, and this is like if the husband were insane, for his insanity does not prevent his wife from requesting separation under the provisions of the law. It is established legally and jurisprudentially that the insane is like a non-discerning minor, as stated in Article 979 of the Majalla, and Article 47 of the new Civil Code that replaced it. [End]
Source pages p. 757

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam discusses the third stage of legal capacity—the stage of discernment (tamyiz). The author begins by stating a ruling: the divorce of a discerning minor is void. The reason given is that the minor loses the mahr and the benefit of marriage through the divorce; since the divorce is a harmful act for him, it is not valid. This is a general rule: any transaction that is pure harm (ḍarar maḥḍ) is not valid from a minor. However, an exception is made: a judge may lend the property of an orphan. Although lending initially involves a donation (tabarru'), which would be harmful because it gives away the property, the judge's lending is permitted because it protects the orphan's money from loss. The author explains that lending is better than deposit because a deposit is not guaranteed if lost without negligence, whereas a loan is guaranteed in all circumstances. So the judge ensures the safety of the orphan's property. The footnote elaborates on the implication of the void divorce: it does not prevent a wife from seeking judicial separation (tatlīq) under modern personal status law. The reasoning is that the right to file for separation depends on the capacity of the plaintiff, not the defendant. Therefore, an adult wife can seek divorce from her minor husband, just as she could if he were insane.

Key Terms
الصغير المميز (al-ṣaghīr al-mumayyiz): A minor who has reached the age of discernment (tamyiz), capable of understanding basic transactions, typically around 7 years.
ضرر محض (ḍarar maḥḍ): Pure harm: a transaction that brings no benefit to the minor and causes loss of property or rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a discerning minor's divorce considered void according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because the minor lacks intention
B) Because it causes loss of mahr and marital benefit
C) Because the minor is not Muslim
D) Because divorce requires witnesses

Key Takeaway
A discerning minor's harmful acts are void, but a judge may lend orphan property to guarantee its safety, exemplifying the Hanafi principle of balancing harm and benefit in legal capacity.

Framework for Discerning Minor's Transactions with Guardian Consent
Rulings on transactions of a discerning minor that are potentially beneficial or harmful

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on a discerning minor's transaction with guardian consent, including the effect of excessive loss and the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
A discerning minor may transact, but binding requires guardian consent; Abu Hanifa permits even excessive loss if consented without deception.

This passage from the chapter on legal capacity explores the third type of transactions for a discerning minor: those that are potentially beneficial or harmful. It explains that while the minor has the capacity to perform such transactions, their binding effect depends on the consent of the legal representative. The passage also delves into an important intra-madhhab dispute regarding excessive loss (ghubn fahish) when the minor has been given prior permission to trade.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of transactions
The passage categorizes a discerning minor's transactions into three types: purely beneficial, purely harmful, and potentially beneficial or harmful. This lesson focuses on the third type, which includes sales, leases, pledges, etc.
Purely beneficial or harmful transactions have different rulings not covered here.
2
State the requirement for enforcement
For the third type, the minor's transaction is valid in itself (sahih) but not enforceable (nâfidh) without the approval of the legal representative (guardian or executor).
Validity refers to the contract's formation; enforcement requires capacity and authority.
3
Distinguish prior permission from subsequent ratification
The guardian's consent can be prior permission (idhn) or subsequent ratification (ijaza). With prior permission, the transaction is binding from the start; with ratification, it becomes binding retroactively.
Both have the same legal effect—binding the minor—but at different times.
4
Analyze the dispute on excessive loss
If the minor had prior permission, Imam Abu Hanifa holds the transaction binding even if it involves excessive loss (ghubn fahish), unless there is deception. His two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not allow excessive loss against a permitted minor. The passage notes Abu Hanifa's view is preponderant.
The dispute is only about the binding effect when loss is excessive; the minor's capacity is not questioned.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is Abu Hanifa's view: the transaction is binding even with ghubn fahish, as trade involves mutual loss and the minor may consent for commercial reasons.
This is based on the authority of Radd al-Muhtar and the tarjih within the madhhab.

۸۰۸
Chapter (62) The third stage of legal capacity: the stage of discernment. Even without the permission of his guardian or executor - includes training, correction of his statements, and introduction to types of transactions, their methods and results as much as possible, all at the responsibility and risk of the principal, and this is pure benefit for the minor. (See: Jami' Ahkam al-Sighar, first chapter of issues of agency, and Radd al-Muhtar 4/400). 62/9 - The third type: transactions that are potentially beneficial or harmful: such as financial compensations in all their forms: sale and purchase, lease and hire, pledge and taking pledge, sharecropping, musaqat, and everything that admits profit and loss. This type of transaction, the discerning minor is capable of undertaking, and it is valid, but he does not have independent discretion; rather, for its execution, the approval of his legal representative is necessary. (See: Majalla article 967, as previously indicated in the discussion of execution among the general effects of contracts, F 5/38). The validity of the discerning minor directly undertaking these transactions is because he possesses capacity of performance, but for their execution, the approval of the legal representative (guardian or executor) is required due to the deficiency of that capacity in the minor and the potential benefit or harm in these transactions concerning him. If they are coupled with the approval of his legal representative (guardian or executor), they are deemed in the minor's interest and are binding on him as if the representative himself had undertaken them. 62/10 - The approval of the legal representative is realized in one of two forms: either prior permission before the minor's transaction, or subsequent ratification after the transaction. (1) By 'legal representative' here is meant whoever has financial guardianship over the minor. The explanation of the guardian of property and the guardian of person and their powers will come in Chapter 66, discussing legal guardianship arising from deficiency of capacity. (2) Permission for the discerning minor to engage in financial transactions and trade is beneficial training, giving him practice, experience, knowledge of people's conditions and results of transactions; it is preparation for his maturity and testing of his intellectual talents. Its basis is the saying of Allah the Exalted in Surah an-Nisa: 'And test the orphans until they reach the age of marriage; if you perceive in them sound judgment, then hand over to them their property.' [Surah an-Nisa 4:6] A.Y 1 [PAGE_760] Book Eight: The Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship A. If the discerning minor undertakes any transaction of this type with prior permission from his legal representative, the transaction is valid, executed, and binding on the minor from the moment it issues from him, even if it contains excessive loss (ghubn fahish) against him, provided the loss is not a result of deception. This is because trade is never free of mutual loss, and consent to loss may be a method of commercial advertising to attract hearts and earn more profit (see: Radd al-Muhtar, Kitab al-Ma'dhun, 5/100). This is the opinion of Abu Hanifa, and it is the preponderant opinion in his madhhab, contrary to the opinion of his two companions, who do not allow excessive loss against a discerning minor even if the minor is permitted. B. If he undertakes it without prior permission, the transaction is valid but not executed; rather, it is suspended upon the ratification of his legal representative. If his representative ratifies it, it becomes executed from the time it occurred, i.e., it takes effect retroactively, because subsequent ratification is like prior permission (see: F 2/38). If he rejects it, it is considered void from the time it occurred as well. From this type is the marriage contract: if a discerning minor marries without the permission of his guardian for marriage, the contract is suspended upon his ratification (see: Personal Status law of Qadri Pasha, article 493). W - Rulings on permission for the minor to transact 62/11 - Scope of permission for the discerning minor to engage in trade: If the legal representative permits the minor to practice trade, the permission includes all transactions that are customarily considered trade matters, such as sale, lease, sharecropping, pledge, taking pledge, agency, etc., and also acknowledgment of rights. And 'test' means examination. He commanded training them and testing their ability to transact before puberty, so that when they reach puberty it is seen whether this has brought them sound judgment. If their sound judgment appears after puberty, their capacity is completed and their property is handed over to them; otherwise, sound judgment is awaited, and its explanation will come. 808
Source pages p. 759

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the third type of transactions for a discerning minor (saghir mumayyiz): those that are potentially beneficial or harmful, such as sales, leases, pledges, etc. The text states that such transactions are valid in themselves because the minor has the capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-ada'), but execution (nufadh) requires the approval of the legal representative (guardian or executor) due to the deficiency of capacity. The Hanafi position: the minor's own direct action is valid, but not binding until the guardian consents. The guardian's consent can be prior permission (idhn) or subsequent ratification (ijaza). The text then details the effect: with prior permission, the transaction is binding even if there is excessive loss (ghubn fahish), according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because trade involves mutual loss and the minor might consent for commercial reasons. His two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree and do not allow excessive loss against a permitted minor. The author notes that Abu Hanifa's view is the preponderant (rajih) in the madhhab. Without prior permission, the transaction is suspended (mawquf) upon the guardian's ratification; ratification makes it effective retroactively, while rejection makes it void from the start. An example from marriage is given, where a minor's marriage without guardian's permission is suspended. Finally, the scope of a general permission for trade includes all customary trade activities and also acknowledgments of rights. The passage also mentions that the permission is based on training the minor, as per Quran 4:6.

Key Terms
تمييز (tamyiz): The stage of discernment in legal capacity, where a child can understand the nature of transactions but still has deficient capacity.
غبن فاحش (ghubn fahish): Excessive loss or price disparity in a transaction, where the value difference is significant beyond normal market fluctuation.
إذن (idhn): Prior permission given by the legal representative to the minor allowing the transaction.
إجازة (ijaza): Subsequent ratification of a transaction by the legal representative after it has occurred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of a discerning minor purchasing an item with prior permission but with a 30% excessive loss (no deception)?
A) The purchase is void due to excessive loss.
B) The purchase is binding on the minor.
C) The purchase is suspended pending ratification.
D) The minor has the option to cancel the purchase.

Key Takeaway
The key takeaway is that a discerning minor's transaction with guardian consent is binding; even excessive loss does not void it according to the preponderant Hanafi view, absent deception.

Distinction Between Authorization and Usage of Discerning Minor
Ruling distinguishing idhn and istikhdam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general authorization (idhn) and specific usage (istikhdam) and apply the rule to determine when a discerning minor becomes an authorized trader.

Core Idea
Sending a minor for a specific errand does not make him an authorized trader.

In Hanafi fiqh, a discerning minor can be granted permission to engage in transactions under certain conditions. However, not every mundane errand qualifies as authorization. This lesson clarifies the critical distinction between granting general trading authority (idhn) and merely employing the minor for a specific task (istikhdam). Understanding this difference is essential for a mufti when evaluating the minor's legal capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
What is the guardian's action? Is it giving general permission to trade or a specific instruction?
2
Classify the permission
Determine if the permission is for a generic type of transaction (naw'i) or a specific one-time purchase (shakhsi).
Generic = repeating, e.g., 'buy and sell' vs specific 'buy this bread'
3
Apply the rule
If generic, the minor becomes ma'dhun (authorized). If specific, it is only istikhdam and does not authorize.
4
Check consequences
If wrongly considered authorization, it closes the door of utilizing minors for errands.
Reference: Al-Majalla 969

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٥/٦٢ - التمييز بين الإذن بالتصرف وبين الاستخدام: ولكن على كل حال يجب التمييز بين الإذن والاستخدام، فلا يتحقق الإذن إلا بما يفيد صراحة أو دلالة إطلاق الحق للصغير المميز من الشرعي في تصرف نوعي، أي ببعض أنواع التعامل. أما لو أرسله إلى السوق ليشتري له شيئاً معيناً من طعام أو كسوة أو غيرهما، فهذا استعانة واستخدام في تصرف شخصي محدود وليس إذناً في تصرف نوعي متكرر، فلا يصير به الصغير مأذوناً، وإلا انسد باب الاستخدام المجرد (ر: المجلة ٩٦٩ ، والهداية وشروحها كتاب المأذون ،۲۱۹/۸ ، ورد المحتار ٩٨/٥).
15/62 - The distinction between authorization for transactions and usage: In any case, one must distinguish between authorization and usage. Authorization is only realized by what explicitly or implicitly indicates granting the right to the discerning minor from his legal representative to engage in a generic type of transaction, i.e., some types of dealing. But if he sends him to the market to buy a specific item of food or clothing or the like, that is assistance and use for a limited personal transaction, not authorization for a repeating generic transaction. Thus the minor does not become authorized by it, otherwise the door of pure usage would be closed.
Source pages p. 763

Commentary
This passage clarifies the distinction between two concepts: idhn al-tasarruf (authorization to transact) and istikhdam (usage). The author states that authorization is not achieved unless the guardian explicitly or implicitly gives the minor the right to engage in a certain category of transactions (tasarruf naw'i), meaning a type of dealing like buying and selling generally. In contrast, if the guardian merely sends the minor to purchase a specific item, that is considered assistance (isti'ana) and usage (istikhdam) for a limited personal task, not a general authorization. Therefore, the minor does not become 'ma'dhun' (authorized) by such specific errands. The author warns that if such specific tasks were considered authorization, it would block the possibility of employing minors for simple errands without granting them full trading authority. This is a well-established rule in Hanafi fiqh, referenced from Al-Majalla and other Hanafi sources.

Key Terms
الإذن بالتصرف (al-idhn bi al-tasarruf): Authorization to transact; granting the minor the right to engage in a type of transaction generally.
الاستخدام (al-istikhdam): Usage; employing the minor for a specific task without giving general authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between idhn and istikhdam?
A) Idhn is for specific tasks
B) Istikhdam is for repeated trading
C) Idhn gives general right to transact
D) No difference

Key Takeaway
A minor does not become an authorized trader by merely being sent on specific errands; explicit general permission to engage in a type of transaction is required.

Two Categories of Discerning Minors: Authorized and Restricted
Classification of discerning minors based on authorization status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify discerning minors into authorized (ma'dhun) and restricted (mahjur) and apply the fatwa rule regarding the effect of authorization on their transactions.

Core Idea
Authorization by guardian makes minor's transactions binding; without it, they are suspended pending ratification.

This passage summarizes the key classification of discerning minors (mumayyiz) in Hanafi muamalat. Understanding the distinction between authorized and restricted minors is crucial for a mufti when assessing the validity of a minor's transactions. The lesson establishes the default rule and its scope, clarifying that authorization only applies to mumayyiz, not to non-discerning minors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Discern the two types: ma'dhun (authorized) and mahjur (restricted). The classification is based on whether the minor has been granted permission to trade by his legal representative.
2
Define the authorized minor
The authorized minor is treated as fully capable for transactions that depend on consent. His transactions are binding even if he suffers gross disadvantage.
The guardian's authorization compensates for the minor's deficient capacity.
3
Define the restricted minor
The restricted minor's transactions are suspended (mawquf) on the guardian's ratification. They are not void but become binding only upon approval.
If the guardian refuses, the transaction becomes void.
4
Understand the scope of authorization
Authorization only applies to discerning minors (mumayyiz). For non-discerning minors, capacity is absent and all transactions are void regardless of authorization.
This distinction is often overlooked by students.
5
Apply the rule in fatwa
When encountering a minor's transaction, first determine if he is mumayyiz, then check if he was authorized. Use the classification to decide if the transaction is binding, suspended, or void.
This is a foundational rule for financial muamalat fatwas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٦/٦٢ - والخلاصة : أنه بالنظر إلى الأذن وعدمه يصنف الصغار المميزون إلى صنفين: مأذونين ومحجورين: فالمأذون هو الذي أطلق له ممثله الشرعي حق التعامل في التصرفات التي تتوقف في الأصل على موافقته وهو النوع الثالث المتقدم. وعندئذ الصغير في هذه التصرفات ككامل الأهلية تنفذ عليه وتلزمه ولو كان يصبح فيها غبن فاحش . والمحجور هو الذي لا يزال تصرّفه في هذا النوع خاضعاً لإجازة ممثله الشرعي لقصور أهلية الأداء لديه. ويتضح من ذلك أن الإذن بالتجارة يختص تأثيره بالصغار المميزين. أما الذين دون سن التمييز فأهلية الأداء معدومة لديهم؛ وكل تصرفاتهم باطلة كما تقدم. فالإذن لهم وعدمه سواء.
16/62 - Summary: According to the presence or absence of authorization, discerning minors are classified into two categories: authorized (ma'dhun) and restricted (mahjur). The authorized one is the one to whom his legal representative has granted the right to engage in transactions that originally depend on his consent, which is the third type mentioned earlier. In such transactions, the minor is like a fully capable person; they are binding upon him even if there is gross disadvantage. The restricted one is the one whose transactions in this type remain subject to the ratification of his legal representative due to the deficiency of his performing capacity. It is clear from this that authorization for trade is specific in its effect to discerning minors. As for those below the age of discretion, their performing capacity is non-existent; all their transactions are void as previously stated. Authorization and non-authorization are the same for them.
Source pages p. 763

Commentary
This passage summarizes the classification of discerning minors based on whether they have been granted authorization to trade (idhn) by their legal representative. The author defines the authorized minor as one who has been given the right to engage in the type of transactions that normally require the guardian's consent (the third type of transactions discussed earlier). In these transactions, the authorized minor is treated as fully capable: his transactions are effective and binding on him, even if he suffers gross disadvantage (ghaban fahish). The restricted minor (mahjur) is one whose transactions in this type remain suspended pending ratification by his guardian due to his deficient capacity (qusur ahliyat al-ada'). Furthermore, the author clarifies that the concept of authorization only applies to discerning minors (mumayyiz). For non-discerning minors (below the age of discretion), their capacity is completely absent, so all their transactions are void regardless of authorization. Thus, authorization has no effect on them.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): The authorized minor; one who has been given permission to trade by his guardian.
المحجور (al-mahjur): The restricted minor; one who is not authorized and his transactions are subject to ratification.

D) Voidable

Key Takeaway
Discerning minors are either authorized (binding transactions) or restricted (suspended transactions); authorization is irrelevant for non-discerning minors.

Conditions for Revoking the Authorization of a Discerning Minor
Ruling with conditions for revocation of authorization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a valid revocation of a minor's authorization and explain the difference when the authorization was public versus private.

Core Idea
Revocation must be known to the minor and, if public, to the market.

This passage addresses a practical question for Hanafi muftis: how does a guardian validly revoke the authorization of a discerning minor? The ruling hinges on protecting both the minor and third parties. We will examine the two essential conditions and their implications for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act
Recognize that the passage is about 'hajr al-ma'dhun' – revoking the guardian's earlier permission for the minor to trade. This is a unilateral act by the guardian.
The act is not a punishment but a withdrawal of capacity.
2
Extract the first condition
The minor himself must be informed of the revocation. Without his knowledge, the revocation is not effective.
This ensures the minor knows he is no longer authorized.
3
Extract the second condition (publicity)
If the original authorization was publicly announced or widely known in the market, the revocation must also be announced publicly. Otherwise, third parties might be deceived.
The level of publicity for revocation matches that of the authorization.
4
Understand the prospective effect
The revocation does not invalidate transactions made by the minor before he and the market knew of the revocation. Past deals remain valid.
This protects the integrity of completed contracts.
5
Apply the exception
If the authorization was not public, only informing the minor suffices; no market announcement is needed.
The guiding principle is preventing harm to unaware parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٧/٦٢ حجر المأذون وما يشترط فيه : أشرنا سابقاً إلى أنَّ الإذن بالتجارة للصغير المميز قابل للاسترداد. يحجره بعد الإذن، وأن يعود فيأذنه بعد الحجر، لأنَّ فلممثله الشرعي من يملك منح الاعتبار يملك استرداده ويشترط لصحة الحجر أن يعلم به الصغير نفسه (۱). ثم إذا كان ممثل الصغير قد أعلن إذنه في السوق، أو كان إذنه شائعاً السوق، أو كان إذنه شائعاً يجب أيضاً أن يعلن الحجر ويشاع ليحيط الناس به علماً كي لا يتغرروا فيتضرروا. ولا يصح حجره سراً في الغياب أو بين عدد قليل من أهل السوق، بل يبقى الصغير على حكم الإذن وتنفذ معاملاته إلى أن يعلم بحجره هو وأكثر أهل السوق. أما إذا كان إذنه غير مُعلَن ولا شائع فيكفي في صحة حجزه إعلامه به دون إعلان ( ر ) رد المحتار ١٠٥/٥). ويجب أن يلحظ هنا أن حجر المأذون إنما يقع مقتصراً ليس له استناد - أي تأثير رجعي فإذا حجر في حال إذنه السابق يظل نافذاً. الصغير المأذون فإن ما مضى من تصرفاته
17/62 - Revocation of the authorized minor and its conditions: We previously indicated that authorization for trade for the discerning minor is revocable. He may revoke him after authorization, and he may return and authorize him after revocation, because the legal representative who has the power to grant consideration has the power to revoke it. For the validity of revocation, it is conditioned that the minor himself knows of it. Then if the minor's representative has announced his authorization in the market, or if his authorization was widespread, he must also announce the revocation and make it widespread so that people are aware of it, lest they be deceived and harmed. It is not valid to revoke him secretly in his absence or among a small number of market people; rather, the minor remains under the rule of authorization and his transactions are effective until he and most of the market people know of his revocation. But if his authorization was not announced nor widespread, then for the validity of his revocation, informing him alone is sufficient without public announcement. It must be noted here that the revocation of the authorized minor only applies prospectively, not retroactively. So if he is revoked, the previous transactions of the authorized minor remain effective.
Source pages p. 764

Commentary
This passage details the conditions for revoking the authorization of a discerning minor (hajr al-ma'dhun). The author first states that the guardian has the authority to revoke the authorization because he granted it. For the revocation to be valid, two conditions are mentioned: (1) The minor himself must be informed of the revocation. (2) If the authorization was publicly announced or well-known in the market, the revocation must also be announced publicly to prevent people from being misled. If the revocation is done secretly, it is invalid and the minor's transactions remain effective until the minor and the majority of market people know of it. However, if the authorization was not public, simply informing the minor is sufficient. An important point is that the revocation only has prospective effect; it does not nullify previous transactions made while the minor was authorized. Thus, past transactions remain valid and binding.

Key Terms
الحجر (al-hajr): Revocation of authorization; the guardian's act of withdrawing the minor's permission to transact.
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): The minor who has been given permission (authorization) to engage in trade or transactions.
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission or authorization granted by the guardian to the minor to engage in transactions.
إعلان (i'lan): Public announcement; making the revocation known to the community, especially the market.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only informing the minor
B) Public announcement
C) Court order
D) Consent of the minor

Key Takeaway
Revocation of a minor's authorization is valid only if the minor knows, and if the authorization was public, the revocation must be public too.

Interpreting Absolute Texts: Puberty Requires Rushd
Critical refinement of fiqh texts and intra-madhhab dispute on rushd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition of rushd implied in absolute Hanafi texts on capacity at puberty, and apply the fatwa hierarchy to distinguish between taklif (Shari' obligation) and ahliyyat al-tasarruf (capacity of disposition).

Core Idea
Absolute fiqh texts on guardianship ending at puberty are conditional upon the person being rashid (sound in judgment).

You may encounter apparently unconditional statements in classical Hanafi works: 'When a child reaches puberty, guardianship ends and full capacity commences.' However, these texts are not to be taken at face value. The same jurists, in other contexts, qualify them by the requirement of rushd (sound judgment). This lesson uncovers that qualification and shows you how to avoid a common misreading that conflates taklif (legal obligation) with ahliyyat al-tasarruf (capacity to manage property). We will also explore the intra-madhhab dispute on the age limit for confirming rushd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the absolute text
Locate a fiqh text that states guardianship ends at puberty (e.g., 'idha balagha al-insanunqata'at al-wilaya'). Recognize that it appears unconditional.
The text is from the source: 'إذ بلغ الإنسان عاقلاً انقطعت عنه الولاية'.
2
Apply the principle of restriction (taqyid)
Understand that such absolutes are interpreted as cases of rashd, based on the Quranic verse (4:5) and Hanafi jurisprudence. The commentator explicitly says these texts are 'mamlula' (interpreted) to the rashid person.
See the given passage: 'محمولة على ما إذا بلغ الشخص رشيداً'.
3
Distinguish taklif from ahliyyat al-tasarruf
Taklif (being subject to commands) begins at puberty and sanity. Ahliyyat al-tasarruf (capacity to dispose of property) requires rushd. The two are separate. Failure to distinguish leads to the misunderstanding that modern laws based on fixed age are needed.
The passage criticizes the Egyptian law for not separating these concepts.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on rushd age
Imam Abu Hanifa held that if rushd is not proven, one waits until age 25. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) held that rushd must be verified regardless of age. Both are valid within the madhhab.
The madhhab dispute map gives the details.
5
Apply the fatwa hierarchy
Determine the mufta bihi (operative opinion) for fatwa. The majority of later Hanafis follow the Sahibayn (wait for actual rushd). However, Abu Hanifa's view is also considered authoritative in some contexts.
The passage implies that the mufta bihi is to wait for actual rushd (majority view), but the 25-year limit is a permissible alternative.

Source Text
