3/64 -- This, and it is necessary to pay attention here to the fact that one may find texts of the jurists that explicitly state that when a person reaches puberty with sanity, guardianship is severed from him, his capacity is completed, and his disposition and acknowledgment are valid (1). But these fiqhi texts and the like (1) are to be interpreted as referring to the case when the person reaches puberty as a rashid (sound in judgment). And their absolute expression in some places is only based on the detail and restriction established in other places: - see Majallat al-Ahkam, arts. 981-984 and 989.

But some states in Islamic countries, such as the Egyptian state, observed that the public interest requires not hastening to give the minor his wealth immediately upon his legal puberty, but rather it is necessary to wait until he reaches his full strength, and they have also raised the age of discernment according to what they saw of the requirements of interests. And the claim that the jurists are unanimous on that is not conceded, for the professor himself has transmitted in the footnote the view of Abu Hanifa on waiting until the age of maturity (rushd) up to the age of twenty-five for handing over wealth, and the view of the majority of jurists along with the two companions of Abu Hanifa on waiting for rushd no matter how old the non-rashid person reaches, as we will see soon. And this is what the Quranic text mentioned earlier indicates, and upon it is the practice. So what the laws of those Islamic countries went to is based on these explicit ijtihads.'

Commentary
Passage type: Clarification and refinement of apparent contradictions in fiqh texts. This passage addresses a common misunderstanding. Some fiqh texts state that upon puberty, guardianship ends and capacity is complete. However, this is conditional upon the person being rashid. The apparent absolute expressions are restricted by other texts. The author then discusses the disagreement within the Hanafi school: Imam Abu Hanifa held that if rushd is not proven, one waits until age 25, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the majority of jurists hold that rushd must be verified regardless of age. The author criticizes modern laws that set fixed age limits, arguing they confuse taklif (legal responsibility) with ahliyyat al-tasarruf (capacity of disposition). He asserts that the laws of some Islamic countries are based on these explicit ijtihads, not on absolute consensus.

Key Terms
التكليف (al-taklif): التكليف هو توجيه الخطاب الشرعي إلى الشخص البالغ العاقل، ويبدأ بالبلوغ
أهلية التصرف (ahliyyat al-tasarruf): أهلية التصرف هي صلاحية الشخص لإجراء التصرفات القانونية كالبيع والشراء والإقرار
الرشد (al-rushd): الرشد هو صلاح الشخص في تدبير ماله وقدرته على التصرف بحكمة

Key Takeaway
A mufti must never take an absolute fiqh text as final; he must check for restrictions (taqyid) in other parts of the school, especially the distinction between taklif and ahliyyat al-tasarruf.

Automatic Liberation Upon Rushd: Principle and Exception
Statement of the rule that reaching the age of majority automatically liberates from guardianship without need for court order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a fatwa applying the maxim that legal interdiction is automatically lifted upon reaching the age of majority, and identify the exceptions that prevent such liberation.

Core Idea
Reaching the age of majority ends guardianship automatically unless a legal impediment exists.

This lesson examines a foundational rule in legal capacity: the automatic liberation from guardianship upon reaching the age of majority (rushd). In modern statutory law, this is fixed at 18 solar years, but the principle is rooted in the Hanafi concept of natural interdiction (hajr shar'i) ending when its cause ceases. We will explore the rule, its conditions, exceptions, and how a mufti applies it in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule
Find the sentence that states the principle: legal interdiction from deficiency is lifted merely upon reaching the age of majority, unless a preventing factor exists.
The maxim is the anchor for the entire fatwa.
2
Note the conditions for automatic liberation
The text specifies three conditions: (1) completing 18 solar years, (2) being of sound mind, and (3) not being under a judicial interdiction for another reason (like prodigality).
Sound mind is a condition; if missing, the rule does not apply.
3
Understand the exceptions
The rule has two exceptions: unsound mind (even after 18) and a prior judicial interdiction (e.g., for prodigality). These are preventing factors (mawani').
The istithna' in the qawida panel clarifies these.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a person's age, mental state, and any prior interdiction, determine whether they are automatically liberated or still under guardianship.
Use the three conditions as a checklist.
5
Connect to fatwa practice
When a mufti is asked about a person's capacity, he must verify age, sound mind, and absence of judicial interdiction. He then issues a fatwa based on the automatic rule, unless an exception applies.
The mufti should be aware of local statutory law (e.g., Syrian Personal Status Law).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٦٤) الطور الخامس للأهلية : طور الرشد
١١/٦٤ - هل يحتاج التحرر من الولاية بعد سن الرشد إلى دعوى
وقضاء ؟
ويجب أن يلحظ في هذا المقام أنه بمقتضى نص المادة /٤٦/ المذكورة من القانون المدني السوري والمادة / ١٦٣ / من قانون الأحوال الشخصية الجديد يعتبر الرشد ثابتاً للشخص حكماً وتكمل أهليته للتصرف، ويتحرر من تحت الولاية أو الوصاية بمجرد إتمامه الثامنة عشرة من عمره، وهو سليم العقل، دون حاجة إلى ادعاء وقضاء بهذا التحرر .
فالشخص الناشيء يكون محجوراً لقصوره حجراً طبيعياً بحكم القانون، فيتحرر في سن الرشد تحرراً عفوياً أيضاً بحكم القانون لزوال الموجب ما لم يكن مختل العقل، أو يلق عليه حجر قضائي قبل ذلك بسبب آخر (كالشفه) يمنعه من هذا التحرر .
فالأصل ارتفاع الحجر الشرعي الناشيء عن القصور بمجرد بلوغ سن
ذلك الرشد ما لم يمنع من مانع .
۸۲۹
Chapter (64) The Fifth Stage of Legal Capacity: The Stage of Rushd (Majority). 11/64 - Does liberation from guardianship after the age of majority require a lawsuit and judgment? It must be noted in this context that according to the aforementioned Article 46 of the Syrian Civil Code and Article 163 of the new Personal Status Law, majority (rushd) is considered established for the person by law, and his capacity for disposition is completed, and he becomes liberated from under guardianship or trusteeship merely upon completing eighteen years of age, while of sound mind, without need for a claim or judgment for this liberation. So the growing person is under interdiction due to his deficiency, a natural interdiction by law; he becomes liberated at the age of majority automatically also by law due to the cessation of the cause, unless he is of unsound mind, or a judicial interdiction is imposed on him before that due to another reason (like prodigality) that prevents this liberation. So the principle is that the legal interdiction arising from deficiency is lifted merely upon reaching that age of majority unless there is a preventing factor.
Source pages p. 779

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of whether reaching the age of majority (rushd) requires a court order to be effective in terms of liberation from guardianship. The author states that under Syrian law (both Civil Code and Personal Status Law), the age of majority is eighteen solar years. Upon reaching that age, a person who is of sound mind is automatically considered mature (rashid) by operation of law, and his legal capacity is completed. He becomes free from guardianship or trusteeship without needing to file a lawsuit or obtain a court judgment. The text explains that the minor was under a natural interdiction due to his deficiency; this interdiction is lifted automatically when the cause ceases (i.e., reaching the age of majority). Exceptions are if the person is mentally unsound or if a judicial interdiction (e.g., due to prodigality) was placed before reaching majority. The concluding sentence is a legal rule: the original ruling is that interdiction lifts upon reaching the age of majority unless there is a preventing factor. This clarifies that the default in modern positive law aligns with the Hanafi principle that puberty with sound mind ends guardianship, though the age is set by statute.

Key Terms
التحرر (al-taharrur): Liberation from guardianship (wilaya) or trusteeship (wisaya), meaning the person gains full independence in managing his affairs.
الحجر الشرعي (al-hajr al-shar'i): Legal interdiction arising from a legal cause (such as minority or insanity) as opposed to a judicial interdiction for cause like prodigality.
الرشد (al-rushd): The age of majority defined by law (18 solar years), at which a person is presumed to have sound judgment for financial matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He becomes automatically free from guardianship but must obtain a court order to manage his property.
B) He becomes automatically free from guardianship and fully capable without any court proceeding.
C) He must petition the court to confirm his rushd before he can manage his affairs.

Key Takeaway
Legal interdiction from minority ends automatically at the age of majority if the person is of sound mind and not otherwise interdicted.

The Fifth Stage of Legal Capacity: Rushd and Automatic Removal of Natural Interdiction
Defining the rule for automatic removal of natural interdiction at majority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rasm rule on the automatic removal of natural interdiction at majority, and distinguish when a court order is required for interdiction based on the reason (insanity vs. prodigality).

Core Idea
Natural interdiction ends automatically at majority; only prodigality requires a court order to restrict.

In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa corrects a common misconception that a court order is needed to release a person from guardianship upon reaching majority. He presents the correct Hanafi rule that natural interdiction (al-hajr al-tabi'i) from minority lifts automatically at puberty (bulugh). However, if the person is insane or mentally deficient at that time, guardianship continues automatically; if he is a sane prodigal, a court order is needed to impose interdiction. This distinction is crucial for muftis issuing fatwas on guardianship and legal capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of interdiction
Recognize that Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between natural interdiction (al-hajr al-tabi'i) arising from minority, which ends automatically at majority, and judicial interdiction (al-hajr al-qada'i), which requires a court order and can only be lifted by a court order.
Natural interdiction is inherent in the status of minority; judicial interdiction is imposed for specific reasons (insanity, prodigality).
2
Understand the default rule: automatic removal at majority
The default Hanafi position is that upon reaching the age of majority (bulugh), natural interdiction ceases automatically. The person attains full capacity to dispose of his property without any need for a court claim or ruling. This is the rule affirmed by the Syrian Court of Cassation.
This is based on the principle that the reason for natural interdiction (minority) no longer exists.
3
Distinguish the two exceptional cases
If the person reaches majority while insane or mentally deficient, natural interdiction continues automatically because the person lacks the mental capacity required for full legal capacity. No new court order is needed. If the person reaches majority sane but is a prodigal (safih), he is considered fully capable unless a court specifically places him under interdiction for prodigality.
The difference: for insanity, the incapacity is inherent; for prodigality, it is a matter to be proven in court.
4
Apply the procedural implication
A mufti should advise that natural interdiction requires no court procedure to be lifted. However, once a judicial interdiction is imposed (whether for insanity, mental deficiency, or prodigality), it can only be removed by a new court ruling showing the cause has ceased.
This is the practical fatwa rule: default freedom, restriction only by court order for prodigals, and automatic continuation for the insane.

الفصل (٦٤) الطور الخامس للأهلية : طور الرشد
وهذا بخلاف ما لو بلغ الشخص سن الرشد وهو سليم العقل، لكنه سفيه مبذر لماله، فإنه يعتبر كامل الأهلية ما لم يحجر عليه قضائياً للسفه. ومتى كان الحجر قضائياً محكوماً به على الشخص الكبير، سواء أكان ذلك بسبب جنون أو عَتَه طارِئَين بعد الرشد أو بسبب السَّفَه، فإنَّ الحجر القضائي السابق لا يرتفع ولا يعود للشخص كمال أهلية التصرف إلا بحكم قضائي مبني على زوال سبب الحجر السابق.
ر قرار محكمة التمييز السورية الصادر في ۸ نيسان ۱۹۳۱ رقم
۸۳۱
.(۳۷
Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. This is what is reasonable, contrary to the previous practice in our judicial and real estate departments that it was required for a person to be released from guardianship or trusteeship, and for his property to be handed over to him after reaching the age of maturity, that he prove his maturity before the judiciary and a ruling be issued for him. But the Syrian Court of Cassation in its recent ijtihad has corrected this issue, ruling that the natural interdiction arising from minority is removed by reaching majority, and the person possesses complete capacity to act without need for a claim or a court ruling (1). As for if a person reaches maturity insane or mentally deficient, then the natural interdiction that was upon him due to minority continues in our Syrian legislation without need for a new judicial interdiction, and this is required by Article 46 of the new Civil Code and paragraph 4 of Article 163 of the new Personal Status Law (2). (1) Indeed, requiring a claim and a court ruling for release from interdiction and guardianship arising from minority leads to a strange result: a person who does not seek to prove his maturity before the court may reach old age and become a leading figure in science or politics while in the view of government departments he is a minor under interdiction. (2) Article 115 of the Syrian Civil Code considers the insane and mentally deficient to be capable of contracting before a judicial interdiction is placed upon them and the interdiction is publicized according to official procedures, unless the state of insanity or mental deficiency is well-known about them at the time of contracting or the contracting party is aware of it. This Article 115 restricts the generality of Article 46, which requires for a person to have complete legal capacity that he has reached the age of maturity enjoying his mental faculties, for the contradictory implication of this text is that a person who reaches maturity not enjoying his mental faculties does not have complete legal capacity. Thus the ruling of Article 115 of the Civil Code is strange, because it rules for the validity of contracts of the insane and mentally deficient before judicial interdiction upon them, despite the proven insanity or mental deficiency at the time of contracting. Therefore Article 163 of the new Personal Status Law, issued after our Civil Code, came explicitly stating the continuation of guardianship over one who reaches maturity insane or mentally deficient without need for a court ruling, and it did not require that his insanity or mental deficiency be well-known. Thus it abrogated the restriction in the mentioned Article 115 and agreed with the generality of Article 46 of the Civil Code. 830: Chapter (64) The Fifth Stage of Legal Capacity: The Stage of Rushd (Maturity of Judgment). This is contrary to the case where a person reaches the age of maturity while sound of mind, but is a prodigal wasteful of his wealth, for he is considered to have complete legal capacity unless a judicial interdiction for prodigality is placed upon him. And once a judicial interdiction is ruled upon an adult person, whether due to insanity or mental deficiency arising after maturity or due to prodigality, then the previous judicial interdiction is not removed, and the person does not regain complete capacity to act except by a court ruling based on the removal of the cause of the previous interdiction. Decision of the Syrian Court of Cassation issued on 8 April 1931, No. 831 (37).
Source pages p. 780

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the theory of legal capacity and guardianship, specifically addressing the fifth stage of legal capacity: the stage of rushd (maturity of judgment). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by presenting the correct legal principle as opposed to the former erroneous practice in Syrian courts and real estate departments, which required a person to prove his maturity (rushd) before a judge in order to be released from guardianship and to have his property handed over. He explains that the Syrian Court of Cassation corrected this by ruling that natural interdiction (al-hajr al-tabi'i) due to minority is automatically removed upon reaching the age of majority, and the person attains complete capacity to act without needing any claim or court ruling. This is the reasonable position. Then the passage distinguishes two scenarios upon reaching majority: (1) If the person is insane or mentally deficient (junun or 'atah), the natural interdiction from minority continues automatically according to Syrian legislation, based on Article 46 of the Civil Code and Article 163 of the Personal Status Law. This means no new judicial interdiction is needed. The author critiques Article 115 of the Civil Code, which had a different rule (only restricting transactions if the condition was well-known), but notes that the later Article 163 abrogated that restriction. (2) If the person is sane but a prodigal (safih) who wastes his money, he is considered fully competent unless a court specifically places him under interdiction for prodigality. Once a judicial interdiction is imposed—whether for insanity, mental deficiency, or prodigality—it can only be lifted by a new court ruling proving the cause has been removed. This is a clear rasm rule for muftis: the default for a person reaching majority is full capacity; only actual insanity or mental deficiency automatically continues guardianship without a court order, while prodigality requires a court order to restrict capacity. The passage also provides practical commentary on Syrian positive law, showing the interaction between Hanafi fiqh principles and modern legislation.

Key Terms
الحجر الطبيعي (al-hajr al-tabi'i): الحجر الذي ينشأ بسبب الصغر تلقائياً دون حاجة لحكم قضائي، ويرتفع بالبلوغ.
الرشد (al-rushd): النضج العقلي والمالي الذي يمكن الشخص من التصرف في ماله بحكمة، ويعتبر شرطاً لكمال الأهلية.
السفه (al-safah): تبذير المال وإفساده، وهو سبب للحجر القضائي على الشخص البالغ العاقل.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

أ) المجنون يستمر حجره الطبيعي تلقائياً، أما السفيه فلا يحجر عليه إلا بقضاء.
ب) كليهما يستمر حجرهما الطبيعي تلقائياً دون حاجة لقضاء.
ج) السفيه يحجر عليه تلقائياً، والمجنون يحتاج إلى حجر قضائي.
د) كليهما لا يحجر عليهما إلا بقضاء.

Key Takeaway
Natural interdiction from minority ends automatically at majority; only a sane prodigal requires a court order to be interdicted, while the insane or mentally deficient remain under guardianship automatically.

Judicial Restriction (Hajr) on the Wasteful Person: Removal Only by Court
Ruling on judicial restriction for the safih and its removal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on judicial restriction for a wasteful adult and apply it to determine when capacity is regained.

Core Idea
A judicial restriction can only be lifted by a judge, not automatically.

This lesson clarifies a key point in Hanafi law of capacity: a person who is of sound mind but wasteful is considered fully capable unless a court imposes a restriction. More importantly, once such a restriction is imposed, it does not dissolve when the person reforms—it requires a new court ruling. This ensures that the state's oversight protects both the individual and society.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling
The passage states that a person who reaches rushd (maturity) with a sound mind but wasteful (safih) is considered fully capable unless a court imposes hajr.
The default is full capacity; hajr is an exception.
2
Recognize the condition for hajr
The hajr must be qada'i (judicial). It is not effective unless ordered by a judge.
Guardians or family cannot impose it.
3
Understand the effect of hajr
Once imposed, the person cannot freely dispose of wealth. The hajr remains until lifted.
It is a protective measure.
4
Learn the removal rule
The hajr is not removed automatically. It requires a new court ruling confirming that the cause (foolishness, insanity, etc.) has ended.
The text explicitly states 'لا يرتفع إلا بحكم قضائي'.
5
Apply to a scenario
A safih person reforms but does not get a court order. Capacity is still restricted. Any transaction without court permission is invalid.
This is the practical consequence.

Whenever a judicial restriction is ruled upon an adult, whether due to insanity or sudden imbecility after maturity, or due to foolishness, then the prior judicial restriction is not removed, nor does the person regain full capacity to act except by a judicial ruling based on the removal of the prior cause of restriction. Decision of the Syrian Court of Cassation issued on 8 April 1931, No. 831 (37).

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the fifth stage of legal capacity (rushd). It clarifies that a person who has reached the age of maturity and is of sound mind but is wasteful (safih) is considered fully capable unless a court imposes a restriction (hajr) due to his foolishness. The author then states that once a judicial restriction is imposed—whether due to insanity, imbecility that occurs after maturity, or foolishness—that restriction does not automatically end. It remains in effect until a new court ruling confirms that the cause of the restriction has been removed. This ensures that the removal of restriction is based on judicial verification. The passage refers to a Syrian court decision as a practical example.

Key Terms
الحجر القضائي (al-hajr al-qada'i): The judicial restriction imposed on an adult's capacity to dispose of property due to foolishness or mental incapacity.
السفه (al-safah): Foolishness or wasteful spending of wealth without sound judgment.

Classification of Impediments to Legal Capacity
Introduces and classifies impediments into natural and acquired

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given impediment to legal capacity as natural (samawiyyah) or acquired (muktasabah) according to Hanafi usul, and explain the authority level of the classification.

Core Idea
Impediments are divided into natural (beyond choice) and acquired (through choice); this division is organizational, not directly determinative of rulings.

Having studied the stages and rules of legal capacity in a healthy person, we now turn to impediments that affect it. This passage introduces the classification of impediments into natural and acquired, a fundamental distinction in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author also adds a third acquired impediment—indebtedness—based on later fatwa practice. Pay attention to the footnote warning that the classification itself has no independent legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Natural impediments occur without the person's choice; acquired impediments are brought upon oneself through choice.
The Arabic terms are 'samāwiyyah' (heavenly/natural) and 'muktasabah' (acquired).
2
List the natural impediments
Six are given: insanity (junun), imbecility (atah), unconsciousness (ighma'), sleep (nawm), terminal illness (marad al-mawt), and slavery (riqq).
Slavery is a legal status, not a physical or mental condition, but included as a natural impediment.
3
List the acquired impediments
Three are named: intoxication (sukr), foolishness (safah), and added by the author: indebtedness (al-madiniyyah) or bankruptcy (iflas).
Indebtedness was not traditionally considered an impediment; later Hanafi muftis added it.
4
Understand the footnote
The footnote clarifies that the division is merely for arrangement (li-mujarrad al-tartib) and has no direct legal consequence.
Do not assume that natural impediments always suspend capacity fully while acquired ones do not—actual effect depends on the specific impediment.
5
Recognize the evolution in fatwa
Indebtedness was added by later jurists (mutakhkhirin) to protect creditors, reflecting a change in mufta bihi.
This shows how Hanafi fatwa can evolve while preserving the original usul framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنَّ ما تقدم من مراحل الأهلية وقواعدها وأحكامها جميع بحسب أطوار نشأة الإنسان إنما هو بناء على الحالة الطبيعية التي يكون فيها الشخص سليم النشأة. غير أنه قد يطرأ على الأشخاص عوارض جسمية أو عقلية، كمرض الموت، والجنون يعتبر لها في أهليتهم تأثير كلي، أو تأثير جزئي ينقص منها نقصاً تتفاوت درجاته بحسب نوع العارض وطبيعته . هذه العوارض يقسمها علماء أصول الفقه إلى قسمين : سماوية، ومكتسبة. فالعوارض السماوية هي ما ليس للشخص اختيار في إيجادها. والمكتسبة هي ما يكون له في تحصيلها اختيار (1) . وبعد من العوارض السماوية ستة عوارض : الجنون، والعته والإغماء، والنوم، ومرض الموت، والرق. ويعد من العوارض المكتسبة: السكر، والسفه. ونحن نضيف على هذه العوارض المكتسبة عارضاً آخر هو المديونية بدين مستغرق أو الإفلاس (2) (1) ليس لهذا التقسيم ثمرة تنشأ عنه في الأحكام، وإنما هو فيما يظهر لمجرد الترتيب. (2) إن فقهاءنا لم يكونوا يذكرون هذه المديونية بين عوارض الأهلية، لأن الدين، ولو مستغرقاً لم يكن يعتبر في أصول المذاهب والنظريات الفقهية مانعاً من تصرف المدين بأمواله، ثم أفتى المتأخرون بأنه مانع، كما سلفت إشارة إليه في الفقرتين 7/15 و 6/38 وسنوضحه هنا قريباً. فلذا وجب عده الآن من عوارض الأهلية.
Indeed, what has preceded of the stages of capacity, its rules, and its rulings all according to the stages of human development is only based on the natural state in which a person is sound in development. However, persons may be subject to physical or mental impediments, such as terminal illness and insanity, which have a total or partial effect on their capacity, diminishing it to varying degrees according to the type and nature of the impediment. These impediments are divided by scholars of usul al-fiqh into two types: natural (samawiyyah) and acquired (muktasabah). Natural impediments are those for which the person has no choice in their occurrence. Acquired impediments are those for which he has a choice in their acquisition (1). Then among the natural impediments are six: insanity, imbecility, unconsciousness, sleep, terminal illness, and slavery. Among the acquired impediments are: intoxication and foolishness. And we add to these acquired impediments another one: indebtedness with a debt covering all assets or bankruptcy (2). (1) This division has no resulting practical consequence in rulings; it is, apparently, only for arrangement. (2) Our jurists did not used to mention this indebtedness among the impediments of capacity, because debt, even if covering all assets, was not considered in the principles of the schools and juristic theories to be a preventer of the debtor's disposal of his assets. Then the later jurists gave fatwa that it is a preventer, as previously indicated in paragraphs 7/15 and 6/38, and we will explain it here shortly. Therefore, it must now be counted among the impediments of capacity.
Source pages p. 782

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the impediments (awāriḍ) that affect legal capacity (ahliyyah). The author begins by noting that the previous discussion assumed a natural, healthy development. Then he explains that physical or mental impediments can reduce capacity partially or totally. He then presents the classification by uṣūl al-fiqh scholars: natural impediments (samāwiyyah), where the person has no choice, and acquired impediments (muktasabah), where the person plays a role in acquiring them. The natural impediments include six: insanity (junūn), imbecility (ʻatah), unconsciousness (ighmāʼ), sleep (nawm), terminal illness (marḍ al-mawt), and slavery (riqq). The acquired impediments are intoxication (sukr) and foolishness (safah). The author adds indebtedness (al-madīniyyah) or bankruptcy (iflas) as an acquired impediment. The footnotes clarify that this division is merely organizational with no direct legal effect, and that indebtedness was not traditionally considered an impediment but later fatwas treat it as such because it prevents the debtor from freely disposing of wealth. This reflects evolution in Hanafi fatwa practice.

Key Terms
العوارض السماوية (al-‘awāriḍ al-samāwiyyah): Impediments to legal capacity that occur without the person's choice, such as insanity, sleep, and terminal illness.
العوارض المكتسبة (al-‘awāriḍ al-muktasabah): Impediments that a person brings upon themselves through choice, such as intoxication and foolishness.
المديونية بدين مستغرق (al-madīniyyah bi-dayn mustaghriq): Indebtedness where debts equal or exceed the debtor's assets, effectively making the person bankrupt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered a natural impediment (samawiyyah) to legal capacity?
A) Intoxication
B) Foolishness
C) Insanity
D) Indebtedness

Key Takeaway
The classification of impediments into natural and acquired is a conceptual tool in Hanafi usul, with the addition of indebtedness as an acquired impediment reflecting later fatwa evolution.

Classification of Impediments to Legal Capacity
Classification of impediments and a general qā‘ida

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify impediments to legal capacity as either removing or diminishing, and apply the Hanafi qā‘ida that maps each type to an analogous developmental stage.

Core Idea
An impediment either removes capacity entirely (like childhood) or diminishes it (like discernment).

Legal capacity is the foundation of liability. But when a person suffers from an impediment such as insanity or deathbed illness, their capacity is affected. This lesson introduces the Hanafi classification of these impediments and the general principle that governs their legal effect, a key tool for a mufti to determine the validity of transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of impediments
Read the passage and note the two categories: impediments that completely remove ahliyyat al-adā’ (e.g., junūn) and those that merely diminish it (e.g., maraḍ al-mawt).
The text uses 'yuzīl' (removes) and 'yantaquṣ' (diminishes).
2
Understand the effect of each type
Complete removal means the person's legal acts are void (bāṭil). Diminution means the person retains limited capacity, similar to a discerning minor.
A discerning minor (mumayyiz) can perform some acts validly but not others.
3
Grasp the general principle (qā‘ida)
The passage states: the removing impediment brings the person back to the stage of childhood; the diminishing impediment brings them back to the stage of tamyīz. This analogy determines the rulings.
This is a wustā (intermediate) qā‘ida.
4
Apply the principle to a concrete case
Practice: For an insane person, all transactions are void (like a non-discerning child). For a deathbed patient, gifts are restricted (like a discerning minor).
Detailed rulings are in furū‘ works, but the principle guides the analysis.
5
Recognize the scope and limitations
The passage notes that detailed effects are in other books; this principle is a foundation for understanding those rulings.
A mufti must know this classification before delving into specifics.

أحكام
وإن لكل عارض من عوارض الأهلية تأثيراً خاصاً تنشأ عنه استثنائية لتصرفات الأشخاص الذين يعتريهم العارض، تستثنى من الأحكام الشرعية العامة التي تسري على سواهم:

Chapter 65: Impediments to Legal Capacity
Rulings
Each impediment of legal capacity has a specific effect that creates an exceptional status for the actions of persons affected by the impediment, excepted from the general legal rulings that apply to others.
Some of these impediments completely remove legal capacity (ahliyyat al-ada'), such as insanity; and some diminish it, leaving it restricted in scope, such as deathbed illness.
The general principle regarding the effect of each of these two types of impediments is that the impediment that removes capacity brings the person back to the equivalent of the earlier stage of childhood; and the impediment that diminishes capacity brings the person back to the equivalent of the stage of discernment (tamyiz), so the rulings of a discerning minor (mumayyiz) apply to him.
3/65 - This is a definition, classification, and general survey of impediments to legal capacity. As for detailing the effects and rulings of each of these impediments, it is beyond the scope of our discussion here; it is expounded in books of usul al-fiqh and furu'. The books of usul mention them in the discussion of legal capacity and its impediments under the topic of the subject of the ruling (al-mahkum 'alayh) — i.e., the legally responsible person addressed by legal rulings — and the books of furu' mention them scattered in various chapters of fiqh, such as the book of purification, prayer, divorce, interdiction, and others.
Moreover, the scholar Zayn al-'Abidin Ibn Nujaym has also explained many of them in the section on rulings (Fann al-Ahkam) of his book al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, which is the third part of it.
However, we will single out two of these impediments for some clarification below, namely deathbed illness (maraḍ al-mawt) from the divine impediments, and indebtedness (al-madyuniyya) from the acquired impediments.
(1) The term 'person' in Islamic legal terminology is described as 'subject of the ruling' because he is subject to the binding legal rulings that the Lawgiver obligates him to.
Source pages p. 784

Commentary
This passage is a general classification of ‘awāriḍ al-ahliyya (impediments to legal capacity) in Hanafi fiqh. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, first states that each impediment has a specific effect that creates an exceptional status for the legal acts of the affected person, exempting them from the general rules. He then classifies impediments into two types: those that completely remove ahliyyat al-ada’ (capacity to perform legal acts), like insanity (junūn), and those that merely diminish it, leaving it restricted in scope, like deathbed illness (maraḍ al-mawt). The general principle is that a person whose capacity is completely removed is analogous to a child (sabiyy) in terms of legal effects, while a person whose capacity is diminished is analogous to a discerning minor (mumayyiz). This principle guides how their transactions are treated: for example, a mentally ill person’s contracts are void, while a person in deathbed illness can make valid inter vivos gifts but with limitations (e.g., cannot exceed one-third of estate). The author notes that detailed rulings are found in usul al-fiqh and furu’ works, and that Ibn Nujaym compiled many in his al-Ashbah wa al-Naza’ir. The passage sets the stage for a focused discussion on two specific impediments: deathbed illness and indebtedness.

Key Terms
عارض الأهلية (‘āriḍ al-ahliyya): An impediment that affects a person's legal capacity, either removing it completely or diminishing it.
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-adā’): The legal capacity to perform acts that produce legal effects; it is removed entirely by insanity and diminished by deathbed illness.
عارض مزيل (‘āriḍ muzīl): An impediment that completely removes ahliyyat al-adā’, e.g., insanity.
عارض منتقص (‘āriḍ munaqiṣ): An impediment that diminishes ahliyyat al-adā’ but does not remove it, e.g., deathbed illness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the general principle stated, what is the legal status of a person in deathbed illness (maraḍ al-mawt) analogous to?
A) A child (sabiyy) without any capacity
B) A discerning minor (mumayyiz) with limited capacity
C) A completely healthy adult
D) An insane person

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle: impediments that remove capacity liken the person to a child; those that diminish capacity liken them to a discerning minor.

Definition and Legal Theory of Deathbed Illness (Maraḍ al-Mawt)
Defines maraḍ al-mawt with conditions and legal terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi definition of maraḍ al-mawt to distinguish it from chronic illness and state the legal effect on capacity.

Core Idea
Deathbed illness is legally defined by inability to work and connection to death, not by bedriddenness or duration alone.

This lesson unpacks the precise Hanafi definition of maraḍ al-mawt, a key concept in inheritance and gifts law. Unlike common understanding, the Hanafi school sets clear temporal and functional criteria. Mastering this definition will allow you to correctly identify when a person's transactions become restricted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three criteria of maraḍ al-mawt
The definition has three parts: inability to perform usual work, connection to death, and duration of less than a full year without increase. Focus on each element.
The inability is gendered: men outside home, women inside.
2
Apply the 'no increase over one year' condition
If the illness continues for a full year without worsening, it becomes chronic (muzmin) and is treated legally as health.
A static condition loses deathbed status after one year.
3
Understand the progressive exception
If the illness is increasing, even slowly, it is considered deathbed from the start, even if it lasts many years.
Progression, not duration, is decisive in this case.
4
Grasp the technical legal usage
When Hanafi jurists say 'sick' (marīḍ) absolutely, they specifically mean one in deathbed illness; 'healthy' (ṣaḥīḥ) includes both truly healthy and those with non-deathbed illness.
This is a shift from linguistic to technical meaning.
5
Recognize the legal consequence
Deathbed illness restricts legal capacity (ahliyya) similar to interdiction (ḥajr), affecting the validity of gifts and bequests.
Full detail on effects comes in later lessons.

Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship
4/65 - Deathbed Illness: Its Definition and Theory
Definition:
Deathbed illness is the illness that renders a man unable to perform his usual work outside the home, and renders a woman unable to perform her usual work inside the home, and death is connected to it, without it continuing for a complete year in a single state without increase.
There is no difference whether he is bedridden or not, nor whether he dies from that same illness or from another cause during the illness... If death is not connected to it, but rather he recovers and then dies, the previous illness is not considered deathbed illness; its ruling is like that of health.
Similarly, if it continues for a year or more in a single state without increasing, then it is considered a chronic condition, whose ruling is like that of health. However, if it is increasing, even slowly and slightly, it is considered deathbed illness from its beginning, even if it lasts many years.
And if it continues for a year or more without increase, then increases and death is connected to it, it is considered deathbed illness from the date of its intensification (Majalla, Art. 1595).
It is noted in legal terminology that whenever jurists use the words 'sick' (marīḍ) and 'healthy' (ṣaḥīḥ), they mean by 'sick' one who is in deathbed illness, and by 'healthy' one who is not in deathbed illness, whether he is not ill at all or is ill with a non-deathbed illness. Thus, when used absolutely, they mean the legal illness and health that differ in rulings, not their linguistic meanings.
This is an instance of applying the more general to the more specific in the case of 'sick', and applying the more specific to the more general in the case of 'healthy'.
Theory of Deathbed Illness:
The legal theory of deathbed illness is based on the consideration that this...
Source pages p. 785

Commentary
This passage provides the Hanafi definition and legal theory of maraḍ al-mawt (deathbed illness). The definition has three key elements: (1) inability to perform usual work—for men outside the home, for women inside; (2) the illness is connected to death (i.e., the person dies while still ill); (3) the illness does not last a full year in a static condition without increase. If the illness lasts a year or more without worsening, it is considered chronic and treated like health. But if it is progressive, even slowly, it is deathbed illness from the start. The Majalla codifies this (Art. 1595). An important subtlety: in legal terminology, when jurists say 'sick', they specifically mean one in deathbed illness; 'healthy' includes both the truly healthy and those with non-deathbed illnesses. This technical usage affects many rulings, such as the validity of gifts and bequests. The theory is based on the idea that deathbed illness is akin to a legal impediment that restricts capacity, similar to interdiction (ḥajr). The practical result: transactions that are detrimental to heirs (e.g., gifts exceeding one-third) are restricted.

Key Terms
مرض الموت (maraḍ al-mawt): An illness that prevents a person from performing usual work, connected to death, and not lasting a full year without increase.
مرض مزمن (maraḍ muzmin): A chronic illness lasting a year or more without increase, treated legally as health.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi jurisprudence, if a person is ill for two years with a condition that remains static (no increase) and then dies from it, when is it considered deathbed illness?
A) From the beginning of the illness
B) From the date of intensification if it worsened
C) It is never considered deathbed illness because it lasted more than a year without increase
D) Only in the last year before death

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, deathbed illness is a defined legal state that restricts capacity; it is not every serious illness but only one that meets specific criteria of inability to work, connection to death, and limited duration without progression.

Rulings on Gifts of a Terminally Ill Person
States the rules for gifts of a terminally ill person regarding debts and heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules governing gifts of a terminally ill person to three scenarios: full debt, partial debt, and no debt, and identify the conditions that require creditor or heir consent.

Core Idea
A terminally ill person's gifts are limited: debt must be paid first, then only one-third of the remainder can be given freely, and a gift to an heir always requires consent of other heirs.

This lesson presents the core fatwa rules regarding gifts and bequests made by someone in terminal illness (marad al-mawt). These rules are essential for any mufti dealing with wills and estate planning. The Hanafi school has developed a nuanced system to balance the rights of creditors, heirs, and the dying person's autonomy, drawing from the Prophetic prohibition of bequests to heirs. By understanding the three scenarios, you will be able to advise on the validity of such dispositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the patient's debt situation
Determine whether the patient has debts, and whether those debts cover all assets (دين مستغرق) or only part. This is the first factor that determines the ruling.
2
Apply the full-debt rule
If the debt is fully enveloping, the patient is interdicted from all gratuitous dispositions. Any gift is suspended until the creditors grant permission. This is unanimous among jurists.
Creditors' rights take precedence over the patient's freedom to gift.
3
Apply the partial-debt rule
If the debt does not cover all assets, the patient may validly gift up to one-third of the residue after debt repayment. Any excess is contingent upon the permission of the heirs.
The residue is calculated after paying off debts.
4
Apply the no-debt rules
If the patient is debt-free, two rules apply together: (1) all gifts are limited to one-third of the estate, and (2) any gift to an heir is invalid unless all other heirs consent, based on the Prophetic hadith 'No bequest to an heir.'
The one-third limit applies regardless of the recipient; the heir prohibition applies even if the gift is within the one-third.
5
Trace the hadith evidence
The prohibition of bequests to heirs is derived from the Prophet's words after the verse of inheritance. This is a clear textual evidence (dalil naqli). The one-third limit is based on other evidence, but the passage does not cite it explicitly.
The hadith is from Sunan al-Tirmidhi and others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثامن : نظرية الأهلية والولاية

وإن كان دَيْن المريض غير مستغرق فإنَّ للورثة وللوصية عندئذ ما يزيد عن وفاء الدين فينفذ تبرع المريض كما تنفذ وصيته في ثلث الباقي بعد وفاء الدين فإن زاد تبرعه على ثلث الباقي توقف الزائد على إجازة الورثة .

«ألا إن الله قد فرض لكل ذي حق حقه، ألا لا وصية لوارث (۱)
وهذا الحكم من قبيل سد الذرائع الذي تقدم إيضاحه في بحث الاستصلاح (ف) (٧/٥ كي لا يُتَّخذ من طريق الوصية لأحد الورثة سبيل لتفضيله على غيره في الحصة الإرثية، فتزرع بذلك أحقاد دائمة أفراد بین الأسرة.
Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. A - If the patient is in debt that is fully enveloping, i.e., covering all his property, then he is considered interdicted from every gift or endowment by agreement of the jurists. If he makes a gift in any form, even by way of sale at a low price (i.e., with favoritism in the price), or endows something from his property, his disposition is not effective; rather, it is contingent upon the permission of the creditors. If they reject it, it is void; if they permit it, it is effective. And if the patient's debt is not fully enveloping, then there is for the heirs and for the will at that time what exceeds the repayment of the debt. So the patient's gift is effective, just as his will is effective in one-third of the remainder after repayment of the debt. If his gift exceeds one-third of the remainder, the excess is contingent upon the permission of the heirs. B - And if the patient is not in debt, then all his gifts, whether gift, endowment, will, or otherwise in any manner, two rules must be applied together: First: that the gift of the terminally ill patient is limited in its effectiveness to one-third of his property. So what exceeds one-third is contingent upon the permission of the heirs. Second: that the patient's gift to one of his heirs is not effective; rather, it is contingent upon the permission of the remaining heirs, no matter how small its amount, whether it comes out of the one-third of the estate or not. That is due to the saying of the Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him, after the revelation of the verse of inheritance: 'Indeed, Allah has assigned to every rightful person his right. Beware, no bequest to an heir.' This ruling is of the type of blocking the means (سد الذرائع) which was previously explained in the discussion of istislah, so that the method of bequest to one of the heirs is not taken as a means to prefer him over others in the inheritance share, thereby planting permanent grudges among family members.
Source pages p. 787

Commentary
This passage presents the legal rules governing gifts and bequests made by a person during a terminal illness (مرض الموت), a critical area of Hanafi fatwa. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, outlines the rulings in three scenarios. First, if the patient's debt covers all his assets (دين مستغرق), all gratuitous dispositions (تبرعات) are suspended and require creditor consent. This is unanimous among jurists because creditors' rights take precedence. Second, if the debt is partial, the patient may validly gift up to one-third of the residue after debt repayment; any excess is suspended on heirs' permission. Third, if the patient is debt-free, two rules apply simultaneously: (a) gifts are limited to one-third of the estate, and (b) any gift to an heir is invalid unless all other heirs consent—based on the Prophet's hadith prohibiting bequests to heirs. The rationale is to prevent favoritism and family discord (سد الذرائع). This is a well-established Hanafi ruling from the Zahir al-Riwayah, though later fuqaha' have nuanced applications.

Key Terms
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): A fatal illness from which recovery is unlikely and which typically leads to death, as defined by the majority of Hanafi jurists.
دين مستغرق (dayn mustaghriq): A debt that encompasses all of the debtor's assets, leaving nothing after repayment.
سد الذرائع (sadd al-dhara'i'): Blocking the means that may lead to unlawful outcomes, used as a legal reasoning to prohibit permissible acts that could facilitate harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The gift is valid up to one-third of the estate.
D) The gift is invalid regardless of consent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school restricts a terminally ill person's gifts to prevent injustice to creditors and heirs, limiting them to one-third of the net estate and prohibiting any gift to an heir without unanimous consent.

beginner
5 min
The Original Ruling: Debt Does Not Impair Disposal Capacity
States the original juristic rule and its rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the classical Hanafi ruling that indebtedness does not diminish a debtor's capacity to dispose of property and explain its basis in the theory of al-dhimma.

Core Idea
Debt attaches to the dhimma, not to property; the debtor retains full capacity to dispose.

This lesson explores the original position of most jurists regarding a debtor's ability to manage his wealth. The key principle is that debts burden the dhimma (legal liability), not the specific assets, leaving the debtor free to transact and potentially recover financially.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the classical ruling: a debtor's capacity to dispose of his property is complete, regardless of debt amount.
This applies even if debts exceed assets.
2
Locate the rationale
Understand that debts attach to the dhimma, not to particular property, so property remains unencumbered.
Dhimma is an abstract receptacle for obligations.
3
Examine the evidence
The text cites this as a foundational juristic view, supported by the principle that property is free until proven otherwise.
No specific Quranic or hadith proof is given; it is a qiyas-based rule.
4
Note the practical wisdom
A person may increase his wealth through economic activity, repay debts, and avoid insolvency.
This encourages financial recovery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٦٥) عوارض الأهلية
٥/٦٥ - المديونية :
الديون التي تركب الشخص مهما كان مبلغها سواء أكانت مستغرقة لماله أم لا، لم يكن لها في أصل النظر الفقهي لدى معظم فقهاء الشريعة تأثير في أهلية المدين للتصرف بأمواله.
وذلك لأنَّ الديون إنما تتعلق بذمة المدين لا بعين ماله فيبقى ماله حراً غير مثقل بحق لأحد. فتبقى أهليته للتصرف فيه كاملة غير منقوصة، فتنفذ تصرفات المدين في جميع أمواله والإنسان بفعاليته الاقتصادية قد ينمي ما تحت يده من مال بحسن الاستثمار والتصرف، فيفي ديونه ويبني ثروة، وينقلب من إفلاس إلى ثراء.
وسيأتي مزيد بسط وتعليل لهذا النظر الفقهي في الجزء الثالث من هذه السلسلة الفقهية تحت الكلام على نظرية «الذمة»
(ر فتح القدير شرح الهداية للكمال ابن الهمام في كتاب الوقف ٥/ ٤٢٤، والإسعاف باب ألفاظ الوقف وأهله ،ومحله ورد المحتار ٣٩٥/٣). لكن الناس على تمادي الزمن، اتخذوا من هذا الحكم منفذاً للاحتيال، وذريعة يلجأ إليها المدينون لتهريب أموالهم، مستفيدين من الحق في التصرف فأصبح المدين بدافع الكيد للدائن يقف أمواله على جهات خيرية أو على ذريته ومن بعدهم على جهة خيرية، أو يهب أمواله لمن يثق بهم من أقاربه أو أصدقائه، أو يبيع أمواله بثمن بخس يحابي فيه المشتري كل ذلك لكي يخرج أمواله عن مُلكه إلى مَنْ يثق بهم كي يمنع الدائن من تحصيل دينه .
فلما لحظ المتأخرون من الفقهاء ذلك، اتجهوا إلى سد هذه الذريعة فأفتوا بأن المدين بدين مستغرق، ولو كان غير محجور عليه من قبل القاضي، إذا تصرف تصرفاً يؤدي إلى تهريب أمواله من وجه الدائنين، كما لو وَهَبَ ووقف أمواله كلها أو بعضها، فإن تصرفه لا يكون نافذاً، بل يتوقف على رضا الدائنين، صيانة لحقوقهم : فإن أجازوه نفذ، وإن رفضوه بطل.
ومعنى هذا أن المديونية أصبحت تنتقص من أهلية التصرف في المدين، وتجعله محجوراً حجراً عفوياً كالصغير المميز، وبذلك أصبحت المديونية عارضاً من عوارض الأهلية.
بهذا أفتى المتأخرون من فقهاء المذهب الحنبلي استحساناً على خلاف القواعد القياسية التي كان عليها الحكم في أصل المذهب (ر : القواعد لابن رجب تحت القاعدة ١١ ص ١٤).
من ثم تبعهم في هذا الإفتاء فقهاء المذهب الحنفي، فأفتى المولى أبو السعود مفتي الروم بعدم نفاذ وقف المدين إلا فيما يزيد عن وفاء الدين أمواله واستصدر بذلك أمراً سلطانياً وسجله في «معروضاته» وأقره فقهاء عصره ومن بعدهم . (ر) ف ٦/١٥ و٥/٣٨ النوع الرابع).
(1) أبو السعود هو محمد بن محمد العمادي من أهالي اسكليب وهي قرية من نواحي بلاد الروم. ولذا يلقب : مفتي الروم. انتهت إليه في حياته رئاسة المذهب الحنفي في عهد السلطان سليم وسليمان وكان المفتي العام للدولة العثمانية، وقاضي القسطنطينية ورئيسها. عاش محترماً معظماً مدة حياته. وكان يجتهد في بعض المسائل وله آراء انفرد بها، وتبعه فيها فقهاء عصره فمن بعدهم وله شعر جيد وتفسير للقرآن مشهور. توفي سنة ۹۸۲ه (ر) شذرات الذهب لابن العماد في وقائع سنة ٩٨٢ ج ۸ ص / ۳۹۸). ومعروضاته المذكورة تتضمن ما أفتى به من هذه الآراء الجديدة، مما فيه تقييد لنص مطلق أو تعديل لحكم فقهي اجتهادي بناءً على اختلاف الزمان ومقتضيات المصلحة، مما عرضه على السلطان فأصدر أمراً إلى القضاة أن يعملوا به، فصار له قوة القانون، كقضية عدم نفاذ وقف المدين المذكورة (ر: ف ٤/٢٩ الحاشية والدر المختار، ورد المحتار كتاب الوقف ٣٩٥/٣ . ومعروضاته هذه مكتوبة باللغة التركية. وقد وضع العالم الألماني بولص هورستير Horster رسالة عن الفقه الإسلامي في : القرن السادس عشر الميلادي وعن معروضات شيخ الإسلام أبي السعود، ونشر هذه الرسالة باللغة الألمانية سنة / ١٩٣٥ وقدّمها لجامعة بون، وطبعت في مدينة شتوتکارد» (ر) کتاب تاریخ آداب اللغة العربية للعلامة بروكلمن الألماني في ص ج۲ /٥٨٠/ وفي الذيل ج ٢ ص / ٦٥١).
(الأصل ج ۲) ٨٤٠
Section (65): Impediments to Legal Capacity
5/65 - Indebtedness:
Debts that burden a person, whatever their amount, whether they consume all his property or not, originally had, in the foundational juristic view of most jurists of the Sharia, no effect on the debtor's capacity to dispose of his property.
That is because debts are only attached to the debtor's dhimma (legal liability) and not to his specific property, so his property remains free and unencumbered by any right of anyone. Thus his capacity to dispose of it remains complete and undiminished, so the debtor's dispositions in all his property are valid. A person, through his economic activity, may increase the wealth in his hands by good investment and management, thereby paying off his debts, building wealth, and turning from insolvency to prosperity.
Further elaboration and reasoning for this juristic view will come in the third part of this juristic series under the discussion of the theory of 'dhimma' (reference: Fath al-Qadeer Sharh al-Hidayah by Kamal Ibn al-Humam in the book of Waqf 5/424, and al-Is'af chapter on wording of waqf and its people and its place, and Radd al-Muhtar 3/395). However, over time, people took this ruling as a gateway for fraud and a pretext used by debtors to smuggle their assets, taking advantage of the right to dispose. Thus the debtor, out of spite towards the creditor, would endow his property to charitable causes or to his descendants and then after them to a charity, or gift his property to trusted relatives or friends, or sell his property at a low price favoring the buyer, all to remove his property from his ownership to those he trusts in order to prevent the creditor from collecting his debt.
When the later jurists noticed this, they turned to closing this pretext and issued fatwas that the debtor with a debt consuming all his property, even if not placed under interdiction by a judge, if he makes a disposition that leads to smuggling his assets away from creditors, such as giving a gift or endowing all or part of his property, then his disposition is not effective; rather it is suspended upon the consent of the creditors, safeguarding their rights: if they approve it, it is effective; if they reject it, it is void.
This means that indebtedness has become a diminishment of the debtor's capacity to dispose, and makes him under a de facto interdiction like a discerning minor. Thus indebtedness became an impediment to legal capacity.
This is the fatwa of the later jurists of the Hanbali school, based on istihsan, contrary to the qiyas-based rules on which the original ruling of the school was based (refer: al-Qawa'id by Ibn Rajab under rule 11, p. 14).
Then the jurists of the Hanafi school followed them in this fatwa. Thus al-Mawla Abu al-Su'ud, the Mufti of the Romans, issued a fatwa that the waqf of a debtor is not effective except in what exceeds the amount needed to pay off his debts, and he obtained a sultanic order for it and recorded it in his 'Ma'rudat' (submissions), and it was approved by the jurists of his time and after them. (Reference: F 6/15 and 5/38, type four).
(1) Abu al-Su'ud is Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-'Imadi from the people of Iskilib, a village in the region of the Romans. Hence he is titled: Mufti of the Romans. During his lifetime, he attained the presidency of the Hanafi school under Sultan Selim and Suleiman, and he was the chief mufti of the Ottoman state, and the judge of Constantinople and its head. He lived respected and honored throughout his life. He exercised ijtihad in some matters and had opinions unique to him, followed by the jurists of his time and after him. He also composed good poetry and a famous Qur'anic exegesis. He died in 982 AH (refer: Shadharat al-Dhahab by Ibn al-'Imad under events of 982, vol. 8, p. 398). His aforementioned 'Ma'rudat' contains these new opinions he issued fatwas on, including restricting an absolute text or modifying an ijtihadi juristic ruling based on the change of time and exigencies of public interest, which he presented to the sultan who then issued an order to the judges to act upon them, so it acquired the force of law, like the aforementioned case of the non-effectiveness of the debtor's waqf (refer: F 4/29 footnote, al-Durr al-Mukhtar, and Radd al-Muhtar, book of waqf, 3/395). These 'Ma'rudat' are written in Turkish. The German scholar Paul Horster wrote a treatise on Islamic jurisprudence in the 16th century CE and on the 'Ma'rudat' of Shaykh al-Islam Abu al-Su'ud, published this treatise in German in 1935, presented to the University of Bonn, and printed in Stuttgart (refer to the book 'History of Arabic Literature' by the German scholar Brockelmann, vol. 2, p. 580, supplement vol. 2, p. 651).
Source pages p. 788

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (rasm) concerning the effect of indebtedness on legal capacity (ahliyyat al-tasarruf). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by stating the original juristic position: debt does not affect capacity because it attaches to the dhimma, not to specific assets. Thus the debtor can freely dispose of his property. He notes that this will be elaborated later under the theory of dhimma.

Then he explains how people began to abuse this rule by fraudulently transferring assets (through gifts, endowments, undervalued sales) to evade creditors. The later jurists (al-muta'akhkhirun) responded by blocking this pretext (sadd al-dhari'ah). They issued fatwas that a debtor whose debts consume his property cannot make dispositions that harm creditors unless the creditors consent. This effectively makes indebtedness an impediment to capacity, placing the debtor under a virtual interdiction akin to a discerning minor.

The passage notes that this fatwa was first adopted by Hanbali later jurists based on istihsan, contrary to the original qiyas. Then Hanafi jurists followed, notably Abu al-Su'ud, the Mufti of the Ottoman Empire, who obtained a sultanic decree that a debtor's endowment is only valid for the surplus beyond the debt. This ruling became law.

The commentary highlights the evolution of fiqh in response to changing social conditions and the use of istihsan and sadd al-dhari'ah to modify earlier rulings. It also illustrates the mechanism of the mufti's role in shaping positive law (qanun) through sultanic orders.

Key Terms
الذمة (al-dhimma): The legal liability or capacity of a person to bear obligations; debts are attached to the dhimma not to specific property.
أهلية التصرف (ahliyyat al-tasarruf): Legal capacity to perform dispositions (contracts, gifts, waqf) with one's property.
عارض الأهلية (arid al-ahliyya): An impediment to legal capacity that is not inherent but arises later, like indebtedness in this ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the classical Hanafi ruling that a debtor can dispose of his property freely despite debts?
A) The creditor has no legal right until judgment.
B) Debts are attached to the dhimma, not to specific property.
C) The debtor may be insolvent but can still be trusted.
D) The law favors debtors over creditors.

Key Takeaway
The classical Hanafi rule: debt does not diminish capacity because it attaches to the dhimma, not to property.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
6 min
The Later Fatwa: Indebtedness Becomes an Impediment to Capacity
Explains the evolution of the ruling and the new fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why later Hanafi jurists restricted the debtor's capacity and describe the conditions under which a debtor's disposition is suspended.

Core Idea
To prevent fraud, later jurists used sadd al-dhari'a and istihsan to suspend a debtor's harmful dispositions.

This lesson examines how social misuse of the original ruling led later jurists to reconsider. They invoked sadd al-dhari'a (blocking pretexts) and istihsan to protect creditors, making indebtedness a genuine impediment to legal capacity. The fatwa of Shaykh al-Islam Abu al-Su'ud and a sultanic decree cemented this change.

Guided Path
1
Identify the social problem
Recognize how debtors abused the original ruling to evade creditors through gifts, waqf, or undervalued sales.
This was a widespread practice (ta'madi al-zaman).
2
Recognize the legal tools used
Note that later jurists applied sadd al-dhari'a (blocking pretexts) and istihsan (juristic preference) to modify the rule.
These are accepted methods in Hanafi usul.
3
State the new ruling
A debtor whose debts consume his property cannot make dispositions that harm creditors unless creditors consent.
If creditors approve, the disposition becomes valid; otherwise it is void.
4
Trace the school adoption
The Hanbali school first issued this fatwa; then Hanafis followed, notably Abu al-Su'ud.
Abu al-Su'ud obtained a sultanic order making it binding law.
5
Understand the practical effect
This ruling makes indebtedness an 'arid al-ahliyya' (impediment to capacity), equating the debtor to a discerning minor under interdiction.
The original principle of dhimma is not overturned; it is restricted in cases of abuse.

Source Text

(الأصل ج ۲) ٨٤٠
Section (65): Impediments to Legal Capacity
5/65 - Indebtedness:
Debts that burden a person, whatever their amount, whether they consume all his property or not, originally had, in the foundational juristic view of most jurists of the Sharia, no effect on the debtor's capacity to dispose of his property.
That is because debts are only attached to the debtor's dhimma (legal liability) and not to his specific property, so his property remains free and unencumbered by any right of anyone. Thus his capacity to dispose of it remains complete and undiminished, so the debtor's dispositions in all his property are valid. A person, through his economic activity, may increase the wealth in his hands by good investment and management, thereby paying off his debts, building wealth, and turning from insolvency to prosperity.
Further elaboration and reasoning for this juristic view will come in the third part of this juristic series under the discussion of the theory of 'dhimma' (reference: Fath al-Qadeer Sharh al-Hidayah by Kamal Ibn al-Humam in the book of Waqf 5/424, and al-Is'af chapter on wording of waqf and its people and its place, and Radd al-Muhtar 3/395). However, over time, people took this ruling as a gateway for fraud and a pretext used by debtors to smuggle their assets, taking advantage of the right to dispose. Thus the debtor, out of spite towards the creditor, would endow his property to charitable causes or to his descendants and then after them to a charity, or gift his property to trusted relatives or friends, or sell his property at a low price favoring the buyer, all to remove his property from his ownership to those he trusts in order to prevent the creditor from collecting his debt.
When the later jurists noticed this, they turned to closing this pretext and issued fatwas that the debtor with a debt consuming all his property, even if not placed under interdiction by a judge, if he makes a disposition that leads to smuggling his assets away from creditors, such as giving a gift or endowing all or part of his property, then his disposition is not effective; rather it is suspended upon the consent of the creditors, safeguarding their rights: if they approve it, it is effective; if they reject it, it is void.
This means that indebtedness has become a diminishment of the debtor's capacity to dispose, and makes him under a de facto interdiction like a discerning minor. Thus indebtedness became an impediment to legal capacity.
This is the fatwa of the later jurists of the Hanbali school, based on istihsan, contrary to the qiyas-based rules on which the original ruling of the school was based (refer: al-Qawa'id by Ibn Rajab under rule 11, p. 14).
Then the jurists of the Hanafi school followed them in this fatwa. Thus al-Mawla Abu al-Su'ud, the Mufti of the Romans, issued a fatwa that the waqf of a debtor is not effective except in what exceeds the amount needed to pay off his debts, and he obtained a sultanic order for it and recorded it in his 'Ma'rudat' (submissions), and it was approved by the jurists of his time and after them. (Reference: F 6/15 and 5/38, type four).
(1) Abu al-Su'ud is Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-'Imadi from the people of Iskilib, a village in the region of the Romans. Hence he is titled: Mufti of the Romans. During his lifetime, he attained the presidency of the Hanafi school under Sultan Selim and Suleiman, and he was the chief mufti of the Ottoman state, and the judge of Constantinople and its head. He lived respected and honored throughout his life. He exercised ijtihad in some matters and had opinions unique to him, followed by the jurists of his time and after him. He also composed good poetry and a famous Qur'anic exegesis. He died in 982 AH (refer: Shadharat al-Dhahab by Ibn al-'Imad under events of 982, vol. 8, p. 398). His aforementioned 'Ma'rudat' contains these new opinions he issued fatwas on, including restricting an absolute text or modifying an ijtihadi juristic ruling based on the change of time and exigencies of public interest, which he presented to the sultan who then issued an order to the judges to act upon them, so it acquired the force of law, like the aforementioned case of the non-effectiveness of the debtor's waqf (refer: F 4/29 footnote, al-Durr al-Mukhtar, and Radd al-Muhtar, book of waqf, 3/395). These 'Ma'rudat' are written in Turkish. The German scholar Paul Horster wrote a treatise on Islamic jurisprudence in the 16th century CE and on the 'Ma'rudat' of Shaykh al-Islam Abu al-Su'ud, published this treatise in German in 1935, presented to the University of Bonn, and printed in Stuttgart (refer to the book 'History of Arabic Literature' by the German scholar Brockelmann, vol. 2, p. 580, supplement vol. 2, p. 651).
Source pages p. 788

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (rasm) concerning the effect of indebtedness on legal capacity (ahliyyat al-tasarruf). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by stating the original juristic position: debt does not affect capacity because it attaches to the dhimma, not to specific assets. Thus the debtor can freely dispose of his property. He notes that this will be elaborated later under the theory of dhimma.

Then he explains how people began to abuse this rule by fraudulently transferring assets (through gifts, endowments, undervalued sales) to evade creditors. The later jurists (al-muta'akhkhirun) responded by blocking this pretext (sadd al-dhari'ah). They issued fatwas that a debtor whose debts consume his property cannot make dispositions that harm creditors unless the creditors consent. This effectively makes indebtedness an impediment to capacity, placing the debtor under a virtual interdiction akin to a discerning minor.

The passage notes that this fatwa was first adopted by Hanbali later jurists based on istihsan, contrary to the original qiyas. Then Hanafi jurists followed, notably Abu al-Su'ud, the Mufti of the Ottoman Empire, who obtained a sultanic decree that a debtor's endowment is only valid for the surplus beyond the debt. This ruling became law.

The commentary highlights the evolution of fiqh in response to changing social conditions and the use of istihsan and sadd al-dhari'ah to modify earlier rulings. It also illustrates the mechanism of the mufti's role in shaping positive law (qanun) through sultanic orders.

Key Terms
سد الذريعة (sadd al-dhari'a): Blocking the means to an unlawful end; used here to prevent debtor fraud.
عارض الأهلية (arid al-ahliyya): An impediment to legal capacity that is not inherent but arises later, like indebtedness in this ruling.
الاستحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference by which a ruling that deviates from qiyas is adopted due to a stronger reason (like necessity or custom).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for the later fatwa to apply?
A) The debtor must be formally interdicted by a judge.
B) The debt must consume all or most of the debtor's property (mustaghriq).
C) The creditor must have a court order.
D) The debtor must be insolvent for more than one year.

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi fatwa makes indebtedness an impediment to capacity, suspending dispositions that harm creditors until they consent.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Distinguishing ʿĀriḍ from Māniʿ: The Mufti's Rule
Methodological rule for distinguishing ʿāriḍ from māniʿ

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi methodological rule (ḍābiṭ) to classify a given condition as either an ʿāriḍ of legal capacity or a māniʿ / loss of shart, and justify your classification using the criteria from the text.

Core Idea
A true temporary impediment affects either mental faculties or legal authority; everything else is a preventer or missing condition.

In this lesson, we examine a critical distinction for the mufti: how to identify whether a given circumstance reduces a person's legal capacity or merely prevents a specific obligation. The source passage from Dr. Zarqā's work provides a systematic rule (ḍābiṭ) for making this distinction, illustrated with examples like menstruation, forgetfulness, and insanity. By mastering this distinction, you will avoid the common error of confusing non-obligation with incapacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two forms of legal capacity
Recall that legal capacity (ahliyyah) is of two types: capacity of obligation (ahliyyat al-wujūb) — rooted in human nature — and capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-adāʾ) — based on intellect and awareness. Both remain intact in the cases discussed unless a true ʿāriḍ appears.
Childhood (ṣighar) is an original state, not an ʿāriḍ.
2
Distinguish original states from temporary impediments
Childhood and adulthood are original states; forgetfulness, menstruation, insanity are temporary. Forgetfulness (nisyān) is a normal condition that does not diminish capacity but serves as an excuse. Menstruation (ḥayḍ) and postnatal bleeding (nifās) are legal preventers (mawāniʿ), not ʿawāriḍ.
The ḥadīth on forgetfulness shows it is a legal excuse, not an impediment.
3
Apply the ḍābiṭ: does the condition affect mental faculties or legal authority?
The rule states: if the condition affects mental faculties (like insanity, imbecility, fainting) or affects legal authority (like prodigality, death-illness, insolvency in some opinions), then it is a true ʿāriḍ of legal capacity. Otherwise, it is a māniʿ or loss of condition.
The key is whether the condition directly impairs the person's mental or legal competence.
4
Classify examples as ʿāriḍ, māniʿ, or excuse
Menstruation: māniʿ because it does not affect mental faculties or authority. Forgetfulness: excuse (ʿudhr) but not māniʿ or ʿāriḍ. Imbecility: ʿāriḍ because it affects the mind. Childhood: original state, not temporary.
Non-obligation does not equal incapacity; it may stem from missing conditions (e.g., puberty) or existence of a preventer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثامن : نظرية الأهلية والولاية
١ - فالصغر هو الطور الطبيعي السابق من حياة كل إنسان، فهو من الأحوال الأصلية في موضوع الأهلية وليس من العوارض. وقد اعترض بعض علماء الأصول هذا الاعتراض وأجيب عنه بما لا يقنع (ر: كشف الأسرار ٢٦٣/٤).
٢ - والنسيان حالة عادية تطرأ على الذاكرة وليس بآفة تعتري جسم الإنسان ولا عقله ولا وضعه الحقوقي فهو لا ينافي الأهلية في كثير ولا قليل ولا ينتقص منها، ولكنه يعتبر معذرة شرعية تسقط المؤاخذة على إهمال بعض الواجبات الدينية أو الشرائط، رحمة بالناس ورفعاً للحرج لما ورد في الحديث النبوي: إن الله تعالى وضع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان وما استكرهوا عليه(۱) . وذلك كمن نسي الصلاة في وقتها فإنه لا يعتبر مهملاً لها، وكمن نسي ذكر اسم الله على الذبيحة فإنها لا تحرم، وكمن أكل في رمضان ناسياً فإنه لا يفطر .
٣ و ٤ - والحيض والنفاس في النساء موانع شرعية تمنع إيجاب بعض العبادات الدينية التي تشترط لها الطهارة أو تمنع ممارستها. فالحائض والنفساء تتركان الصلاة والصيام، ثم تقضيان ما تركتاه من صيام رمضان دون الصلاة وكذلك تجتنبان دخول المساجد وتلاوة القرآن؛ إلى غير ذلك من أحكام استثنائية أخرى.
لكن أهلية الوجوب وأهلية الأداء بفرعيها الديني والمدني - أي أهلية التعبد وأهلية التصرف - كاملة فيهما .
- أما أهلية الأداء، فلأن مناطها العقل والوعي؛ ولذا تثبت للصغير المميز في حدود قاصرة كما تقدم. وهذا العقل والوعي في الحائض والنفساء متوافر كامل، فلا تأثير للحيض والنفاس في أهليتهما للأداء.
(1) انظر تخريجه في ف ۲/۱۰ ب في الحاشية.
