على أنه يستثنى من ذلك حالة واحدة وهي ما إذا كان النص نفسه حين صدوره عن الشارع مبنياً على عرف قائم، ومعللاً به، فإن النص عندئذ يكون عرفياً، فيدور حكمه مع العرف ويتبدل بتبدله.
وقد تختلف الأنظار الاجتهادية في كون النص الخاص عرفياً أو غير عرفي فيختلف الاجتهاد بناءً على ذلك في اعتبار العرف الطارىء المخالف، وكونه مبدلاً لحكم النص أم لا .
مثال ذلك : مسألة مقياس الأموال الربوية .
فمن المقرّر أنَّ الأموال التي تُعتبر شرعاً من الأنواع الربوية يمتنع فيها
Chapter (71): Collision of Custom with a Specific Legislative Text. And the sale of the hit of the hunter and the diver, (1) and all that was known in the Jahiliyyah, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade it, so it became in Islam prohibited and void. So if the people of a city revert to something of it and make it customary, their custom is not considered. (2) Similarly, the enslavement of the debtor, which was customary among the Arabs of Jahiliyyah and the Romans, Islam came to prohibit it and obliged respite for the insolvent debtor by the explicit text of the Quran in His statement: 'And if someone is in hardship, then postponement until ease' [Surah al-Baqarah 2:280]. Likewise, the custom of Bedouins in shighar marriage is void and not enforceable (see: al-Durar and al-Durr al-Mukhtar in the book of marriage). And this shighar marriage is that two persons agree, each marrying the other's relative; so it becomes the dower for the other. It is customary to this day among Bedouins in our Syrian lands and others. And among their customs is that if one of the two women dies, her husband takes back his relative from the other until he marries him another woman in place of the deceased, as if he is a guarantor for her life! This marriage contradicts the specific texts in the Book and the Sunnah, for they required for marriage a dower that the woman owns, as they established for the woman the right to choose her husband, and prohibited her disobedience or being given away without legitimate cause. 6/71 - The summary is that custom is only operative within the limits of the freedom that the Lawgiver has left to the obligated persons in the fields of transactions and obligations, not in cases where the Lawgiver Himself undertook to specify rulings by way of obligation; otherwise, customs could overturn the foundations of legislation completely, and the Sharia would become extinct and a thing of the past. So the specific text is the one to be considered and respected even if a general custom contradicts it; no custom or agreement contrary to it is considered. 7/71 - Exception of the Customary Text: However, there is an exception for one case, which is when the text itself at the time of its issuance from the Lawgiver was based on an existing custom and reasoned by it; then the text is customary, its ruling revolves with custom and changes with its change. And ijtihad opinions may differ regarding whether the specific text is customary or not; thus ijtihad differs accordingly on whether the opposing emerging custom is considered and whether it changes the ruling of the text or not. An example of that is the issue of the measure of usurious goods. For it is established that goods that are legally considered usurious types are prohibited from
Source pages p. 850

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa addresses a fundamental rule in Hanafi usul al-ifta: the relationship between custom (urf) and specific legislative texts (nusus khasah). The author begins by stating the rule: where a specific text from the Quran or Sunnah exists on a matter, no custom, no matter how widespread, can override it. He gives examples: pre-Islamic sales like munabadhah, mulamasah, and throwing stones (which were forms of gambling and uncertainty) were explicitly prohibited by the Prophet, so even if people later customary revive them, they remain void. Similarly, the enslavement of debtors was prohibited by the Quranic verse demanding respite for the insolvent; any custom to the contrary is invalid. Another example is shighar marriage (exchange marriage without proper dower), which contradicts the Quranic and Sunnah requirement of an independent dower for each woman. The author then distills the principle in section 6/71: custom is only operative within the sphere of freedom left by the Lawgiver; it cannot alter binding injunctions. He then introduces an important exception in section 7/71: if the legislative text itself was based on a prevailing custom at the time of revelation and reasoned by it (nas 'urfi), then its ruling rotates with custom; if the custom changes, the ruling changes accordingly. He cites the example of the measurement of usurious goods (e.g., which weights and measures determine riba) to illustrate this exception, noting that jurists may differ on whether a given text is 'urfi or not. This passage thus provides a key rasm rule for muftis: give absolute priority to specific texts over custom, but distinguish texts that are inherently custom-based and thus subject to change.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Custom: the regular practice of a people that is accepted as a source of law in the absence of a specific text.
النص الخاص (al-nass al-khas): Specific legislative text from Quran or Sunnah that directly and clearly governs a particular matter.
النص العرفي (al-nass al-urfi): A text that was revealed based on a prevailing custom and reasoned by it; its ruling may change with the custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition can an existing custom override a ruling derived from a specific text of the Quran or Sunnah according to this passage?
A) When the custom is widespread and practiced by the majority of Muslims.
B) When the text itself was based on a custom that existed at the time of revelation and is reasoned by it.
C) When the custom is older than the text.
D) When the custom does not contradict a consensus of the scholars.

Key Takeaway
Custom is subordinate to specific texts unless the text itself was based on and reasoned by a custom.

The Scope of Custom and the Exception of Customary Texts
Rule on conflict between custom and specific texts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that custom cannot override a specific textual ruling, but recognize the exception for customary texts and evaluate whether a given text is 'urfi.

Core Idea
Custom only operates where the Lawgiver left freedom; texts based on custom change with custom.

This lesson clarifies a critical distinction for the mufti: when custom collides with a specific text from the Quran or Sunnah, the text prevails—unless the text itself was built upon the prevailing custom at the time of revelation. Mustafa al-Zarqa illustrates this with a harmful tribal marriage custom that violates explicit rulings on dower and consent, and then with the ribawi measurement standards that change as custom changes. Understanding this distinction prevents both rigid literalism and uncontrolled custom-based revision.

Guided Path
1
State the primary rule
Identify the core principle: custom is only valid within the freedom left by the Lawgiver, not where He has determined rulings obligatorily. Recognize that a specific text (nass khas) overrides any custom.
This is the default: texts are authoritative.
2
Identify the exception
Note the one exception: when the text itself, at the time of revelation, was based on a prevailing custom and reasoned by it. Such a text is called 'nass 'urfi' (customary text) and its ruling changes with custom.
The exception is not for all texts, only those that are 'urfi.
3
Analyze the marriage example
The tribal custom where a woman's dower is given to another woman and her husband can reclaim her violates specific Quranic rulings on dower and consent. This custom is invalid because it contradicts a non-'urfi text.
This shows the default rule applied.
4
Analyze the ribawi measurement example
The Prophet's mention of weight and measure for ribawi goods was based on the prevailing custom of the time. When custom changes (e.g., from weight to volume), the standard changes accordingly. This exemplifies the 'urfi text exception.
The hadith did not legally fix the standard; it reflected the existing custom.
5
Apply to fatwa practice
As a mufti, you must first determine whether the specific text at hand is 'urfi or non-'urfi. If 'urfi, you may adopt the new custom; if not, the text remains binding even against a widespread custom.
This requires ijtihad and awareness of the reason ('illa) behind the text.

ومن عُرفهم فيه أنه إذا ماتت إحدى المرأتين يستعيد زوجها قريبته من عند الآخر حتى يزوجه امرأة أخرى بدلاً من التي ماتت، فكأنه ضامن لحياتها ! !

۷/۷۱ - استثناء النص العرفي :
على أنه يستثنى من ذلك حالة واحدة وهي ما إذا كان النص نفسه حين صدوره عن الشارع مبنياً على عرف قائم، ومعللاً به، فإن النص عندئذ يكون عرفياً، فيدور حكمه مع العرف ويتبدل بتبدله.
وقد تختلف الأنظار الاجتهادية في كون النص الخاص عرفياً أو غير عرفي فيختلف الاجتهاد بناءً على ذلك في اعتبار العرف الطارىء المخالف، وكونه مبدلاً لحكم النص أم لا .
مثال ذلك : مسألة مقياس الأموال الربوية .
فمن المقرّر أنَّ الأموال التي تُعتبر شرعاً من الأنواع الربوية يمتنع فيها بيع مقدار منها بأكثر أو أقل منه إذا كانا من جنس واحد، لأن الفضل عندئذ يكون ربا محرّماً، فلا تجوز مبادلة القمح بالقمح مثلاً، ولا الذهب بالذهب، إلا إذا تساوى البدلان.
فإذا اختلف الجنسان كبيع الذهب بالفضة أو القمح بالشعير، جاز التفاضل ولكن تمتنع النسيئة، أي تأجيل أحد البدلين، فيجب التقابض في مجلس العقد (۱).
وهذا من قبيل سد الذرائع، كيلا يتخذ جواز التفاضل عند اختلاف الجنسين ذريعة ووسيلة إلى ربا النسيئة فيستقرض الشخص ذهباً مثلاً إلى أجل ثم يوفي فضة أكثر منه بقدر الربا المراد.
فهذا الحكم كما ترى يقوم على أساس وجوب تساوي الكميات المبادلة في الجنس الواحد فما هو مقياس هذا التساوي، فقد يتساوى المقداران وزناً ويتفاوتان كيلاً، وبالعكس؟
ومن المتفق عليه أنَّ التساوي يُعتبر شرعاً بالمقياس العرفي في كل صنف، فما كان وزنياً عرفاً كالزيت والسمن يجب تساوي الكميتين فيه بالوزن وما كان كيلياً عرفاً يجب التساوي فيه بالكيل.
وإذا تبدل العرف في مقياس شيء فأصبح كيلياً مثلاً بعد أن كان وزنياً، أو أصبح وزنياً بعد أن كان كيلياً تبدل المقياس الربوي فيه تبعاً للعرف .
غير أنه قد ورد النص عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في ستة أصناف من الأموال الربوية (ومنها القمح) أنه يجب التساوي في بعضها بالوزن، وفي بعضها بالكيل (۲)
(۱) يلحظ هنا أن هذه الأحكام الربوية أصبحت غير معمول بها في ظل القوانين الزمنية السائدة في بلادنا وكثير من البلاد العربية والإسلامية فيتبايع الناس اليوم بالأموال الربوية كيفما شاؤوا بمقتضى الأحكام القانونية .
(۲) هي الذهب والفضة والحنطة والشعير والملح والتمر.
Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. So it becomes the dower for the other. And this is customary to this day among the people of the wilderness in our Syrian lands and elsewhere. And among their customs in this is that if one of the two women dies, her husband takes back his relative from the other until he marries another woman in place of the one who died, as if he is a guarantor of her life! This marriage contradicts specific texts of the Book and the Sunnah, for they have mandated in marriage a dower for the woman which she owns, and established for her the right to choose her husband, and prevented her disobedience or being given in marriage without legitimate cause. 6/71 – The conclusion is that custom is only operative within the bounds of freedom that the Lawgiver has left to those obligated in the fields of transactions and obligations, excluding cases where the Lawgiver Himself has determined rulings obligatorily. Otherwise, customs could over time overturn all the foundations of legislation entirely, causing the Sharia to become extinct and become a thing of the past. Thus the specific text is the one respected and authoritative, even if a general custom contradicts it. So no custom or agreement contrary to it is considered. 7/71 – Exception of the customary text: However, from this is excepted one case: when the text itself, at the time of its issuance from the Lawgiver, was based on an existing custom and reasoned by it. Then the text is customary, and its ruling revolves with the custom and changes with its change. Juristic opinions may differ regarding whether a specific text is customary or non-customary, and accordingly ijtihad differs on considering the opposing new custom and whether it changes the ruling of the text or not. Example: The issue of the standard for ribawi wealth. It is established that wealth considered legally as ribawi types is prohibited from exchanging more or less when of the same type, because the excess would be forbidden usury. So it is not permissible to barter wheat for wheat, or gold for gold, unless the two equivalents are equal. If the types differ, like selling gold for silver or wheat for barley, disparity is allowed but deferred payment is prohibited, i.e., postponing one of the two equivalents; thus hand-to-hand exchange in the contract session is required. This is a matter of blocking the means, lest the permissibility of disparity when types differ become a means to deferred usury, so a person borrows gold on credit then repays more silver equal to the intended usury. This ruling, as you see, is based on the necessity of equality of exchanged quantities in the same type. So what is the standard of this equality? Two quantities may be equal in weight but differ in measure, or vice versa. It is agreed that equality is legally considered by the customary standard in each category. What is customarily weighty, like oil and ghee, the two quantities must be equal in weight; what is customarily measured by volume must be equal in volume. If the custom changes in the standard of something, so it becomes measured by volume after being by weight, or becomes by weight after being by volume, the ribawi standard changes accordingly with the custom. However, a text from the Prophet (peace be upon him) has been narrated regarding six types of ribawi wealth (including wheat) that equality in some is by weight and in some by measure. (1) Note here that these ribawi rulings are no longer applied under current statutory laws in our country and many Arab and Islamic countries; people today trade in ribawi wealth as they wish under legal provisions. (2) They are: gold, silver, wheat, barley, salt, and dates.
Source pages p. 851

Commentary
This passage is a methodological rule for muftis regarding the clash between custom and specific textual rulings. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, first establishes that custom cannot override an explicit text (nass khas) from the Quran or Sunnah. He gives an example of a harmful tribal custom in marriage that contradicts explicit rulings on dower and woman's consent. The rule is that custom only operates in areas where the Lawgiver left freedom; it cannot change a determined obligatory ruling. However, he then presents an exception: if the text itself was based on a prevailing custom at the time of revelation and was reasoned by that custom, then such a text is termed 'urfi (customary) and its ruling changes with the custom. He exemplifies this with the standards for ribawi goods: the Prophet (s) specified that some items are weighed and some measured, but the underlying principle is the customary standard. If custom changes, the measure changes accordingly, unless the text is explicitly fixing a particular standard. This shows the mufti's need to differentiate between texts that are fundamentally devotional and those that are based on custom.

Key Terms
عرف ('urf): Custom; recurrent practice accepted by people of sound nature
نص خاص (nass khas): A specific textual ruling from Quran or Sunnah that is clear and definitive
نص عرفي (nass 'urfi): A text whose ruling was based on the prevailing custom at the time of revelation and is reasoned by it
ربا (riba): Usury or interest; illegal increase in exchange of certain commodities

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the exception to the rule that custom cannot override a specific textual ruling?
A) When the custom is widespread and accepted by scholars.
B) When the text itself was based on a custom existing at the time of revelation and is reasoned by it.
C) When the text is from the Quran and not the Sunnah.
D) When the custom is from a later period after the companions.

Key Takeaway
Custom operates only where the Lawgiver left freedom; a specific text cannot be overridden by custom unless the text itself is based on that custom.

Custom vs. Specific Nass: The Ribawi Measures Dispute
Dispute on custom vs. nass in ribawi measures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a specific nass is not overridden by custom, and evaluate the alternative view of Abu Yusuf in fatwa scenarios involving ribawi measures.

Core Idea
A specific nass cannot be overridden by custom, but if the nass itself is based on custom, its application may change.

In this passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa presents a pivotal intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the measure prescribed for the six ribawi items is fixed or subject to change with custom. Understanding this dispute is essential for the mufti when applying ribawi rules in different locales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the view of the majority (Abu Hanifa) that the measure is fixed by nass, and the solitary view of Abu Yusuf that the measure follows custom.
The majority includes the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) but Abu Yusuf uniquely differed on this issue.
2
Analyze the dalil for each
For Abu Hanifa, the nass is specific (khass) and thus takes precedence. For Abu Yusuf, the nass is 'illah (reasoned) by custom; the prescribed measure was customary at the Prophet's time, so it changes with custom.
Abu Yusuf's ta'lil (reasoning) is considered stronger in argument.
3
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider a practical scenario: exchanging wheat for wheat. According to Abu Hanifa, equality must be by volume (kayl); according to Abu Yusuf, if custom has shifted to weight, equality by weight suffices.
The khilaf only applies when exchanging an item for its own genus; cross-genus exchange is free.
4
Note the author's tarjih
The author (Al-Zarqa) leans towards Abu Yusuf's view, stating it is 'stronger in argument and more correct in method' despite his solitary position.
This tarjih is personal and not necessarily the school's authoritative position.
5
Apply to fatwa context
As a mufti, you must decide which view to give as fatwa. The majority view remains operative, but Abu Yusuf's view can be considered in cases of necessity or widespread custom.
Modern markets often use weight; this dispute provides flexibility.

الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
فذهب جمهور المجتهدين ومنهم أبو حنيفة إلى أنه يعتبر في خصوص هذه الأشياء المقياس الذي ورد النص به أبداً ولا عبرة لتبدل العرف فيه .. ففي مبادلة القمح مثلاً يجب التساوي كيلاً لا وزناً ولو تعارف الناس بيعه بالوزن كما في زماننا اليوم (۱) ، لأنَّ هذا نصّ خاص بالمقياس الربوي هذه الأصناف فلا يعتبر العرف على خلافه .
ولكن ذهب أبو يوسف صاحب أبي حنيفة إلى أن المقياس المعتبر حتى في تلك الأشياء الستة هو المقياس العرفي، وأنه يتبدل بتبدل العرف، كما في سائر الأموال الربوية الأخرى التي لم يرد نص خاص بشأن مقياسها ويقول : إنَّ النص في مقياس تلك الأصناف الستة معلل بالعرف، فإنما وَرَدَ النص بلزوم التساوي فيها كيلاً أو وزناً لأن هذا مقياسها المتعارف في عهد النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام . فلو كان العرف فيها على مقياس آخر لورد النص معتبراً فيها ذلك المقياس الآخر (ر ) الهداية وشروحها ١٥٧/٦).
ورأي أبي يوسف هذا كما ترى أقوى حجة، وأقوم محجة، رغم انفراده به .
(۱) ليس معنى ذلك أن يمتنع شرعاً على هذا الرأي بيع هذه الأصناف بغير المقياس المنصوص إذا قوبلت بغير جنسها كالنقود مثلاً، بل للناس أن يتعارفوا في بيعها بخلاف جنسها أي مقياس أرادوا أو بلا مقياس أي جزافاً، ولكن معناه أنه إذا شيء منها بجنسه كقمح بقمح، أو استقرض منها شيء، فإنه يجب عندئذ في الوفاء التساوي بالمقياس الذي ورد به النص. بيع ۹۱۰
Chapter Nine: The Theory of Custom. The majority of mujtahids, including Abu Hanifa, held that in the case of these specific items, the measure that is mentioned in the text is always considered, and no attention is paid to the change of custom... for example, in the exchange of wheat, equality must be by measure (kayl), not by weight, even if people are accustomed to selling it by weight as in our time today, because this is a specific text regarding the measure of these ribawi items, so custom contrary to it is not considered. But Abu Yusuf, the companion of Abu Hanifa, held that the measure considered even in those six items is the customary measure, and it changes with the change of custom, as in other ribawi wealth for which no specific text was revealed regarding their measure. He says: The text regarding the measure of those six items is reasoned by custom; the text was revealed requiring equality in them by measure (kayl) or weight because that was their customary measure at the time of the Prophet, peace be upon him. If custom had been on another measure, the text would have been revealed considering that other measure. (Reference: Al-Hidayah and its commentaries 6/157). And this view of Abu Yusuf, as you see, is stronger in argument and more correct in method, despite his being alone in it. (1) This does not mean that according to this view it is legally prohibited to sell these items by a measure other than the prescribed one if they are exchanged against a different genus, like currency, for example. Rather, people may customarily sell them against a different genus by any measure they wish or without measure, i.e., by guess. Rather, its meaning is that if something from them is sold for its own genus, like wheat for wheat, or if something from them is borrowed, then at that time equality must be observed in payment according to the measure mentioned in the text.
Source pages p. 853

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental dispute in Hanafi usul al-iftaa concerning the interaction between custom ('urf) and specific legislative texts (nass khas). The majority of mujtahids, including Imam Abu Hanifa, hold that when a specific text prescribes a particular measure (such as kayl for wheat) for ribawi transactions, that measure is permanently binding regardless of subsequent changes in custom. Custom cannot override a specific nass. Imam Abu Yusuf, however, takes a more flexible view: even the nass itself was based on the custom of the Prophet's time. Therefore, the measure should follow the prevailing custom, and if custom changes, the measure changes accordingly. Abu Yusuf argues by reasoning (ta'lil) that the nass is 'illah (reasoned) by custom. The author of this text considers Abu Yusuf's argument stronger despite his solitary position. The footnote clarifies that this dispute only applies when exchanging an item for its own genus (e.g., wheat for wheat); when exchanging for a different genus (e.g., wheat for money), any customary measure or even lump-sum sale is permissible. This passage is crucial for understanding the Hanafi methodology of reconciling revealed texts with evolving social practices.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Custom: the established practice of people in their transactions and dealings.
النص الخاص (al-nass al-khass): A specific legislative text from Quran or hadith that explicitly prescribes a ruling.
مقياس (miqyas): Measure: either by volume (kayl) or by weight (wazn) used in exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the measurement of the six ribawi items?
A) Abu Hanifa says all six items must be measured by weight; Abu Yusuf says by volume.
B) Abu Hanifa holds the measure is fixed by nass and not subject to custom; Abu Yusuf holds the measure is based on custom and changes with it.
C) Abu Hanifa says custom overrides the nass; Abu Yusuf says the nass is absolute.
D) Both agree that the measure is fixed, but disagree on whether it applies to non-ribawi items.

Key Takeaway
The dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on ribawi measures illustrates the tension between adherence to nass khass and consideration of 'urf, with Abu Yusuf's ta'lil-based argument considered stronger in principle.

Custom and Change: The Virgin's Consent by Silence
Explains that the hadith on virgin's silence is custom-dependent and changeable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that rulings based on custom change with custom, using the case of a virgin's silence in marriage consent.

Core Idea
The prophetic ruling that a virgin's consent is her silence is conditional on the custom of shyness; when custom changes, the ruling changes.

In this lesson, we examine a key principle in Hanafi ifta: rulings that are built on custom (urf) are not static—they evolve as society changes. The well-known hadith that a virgin's consent is her silence is a prime example. The jurists agree that this ruling is based on the natural shyness of virgins. But if social upbringing changes so that virgins are no longer shy, silence ceases to indicate consent. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this shift and its practical implications for marriage contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Base Ruling
Recall the hadith: 'Idhnuha samatuha' (Her consent is her silence). This is the starting point for the ruling.
2
Recognize the Underlying Custom
The ruling is not self-standing; it is built on the customary shyness (haya) of a virgin. Jurists agree that this is the illah (effective cause).
3
Understand the Conditional Nature
If the custom changes—if virgins become as open as non-virgins—then the illah disappears. Therefore, the ruling must adapt.
4
Predict the New Ruling
When the illah vanishes, silence no longer indicates consent. Explicit verbal consent is required, just as for a thayyib.
5
Apply to Fuduli Contract
Without explicit consent, the marriage becomes fuduli (unauthorized), needing later ratification to be valid.

۸/۷۱ - هذا، ومن النصوص التشريعية الخاصة التي تعتبر - فيما أرى - مبنية على العرف والعادة فيتبدل حكمها تبعاً لتبدل العادة، ما قد أسلفنا ذكره من قول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام بشأن الفتاة البكر البالغة: «إذنها صماتها» ر ف ٩/٦٩ ب) حيث اعتبر سكوتها عند استئذان وليها لها
في تزويجها من رجل معيّن وبمهر معين إذناً منها وتوكيلاً (ر) ف۷/۳ و ١٠/٦٩. فقد اتفقت آراء الفقهاء على أنَّ هذا الحكم في الفتاة البكر مبني على ما هو معروف فيها من الخجل عن إظهار رغبتها في الزواج عند استثمار وليها لها. فعادتها أن تعبر بالسكوت عن رغبتها وإذنها. وهذا الاستحياء لا يزال إلى اليوم غالباً عليهن في معظم الأوساط الإسلامية بحسب التربية والتقاليد الاجتماعية .
فإذا فرض أنَّ هذه التربية قد تبدل اتجاهها وأصبحت الفتيات الأبكار لا يتحرجن من إعلان هذه الرغبة أو عدمها على السواء (كعادة الثيبات غير الأبكار) فإنَّ الإذن منهن عندئذ بالتزويج لا يكفي فيه السكوت؛ بل يحتاج عندئذ إلى بيان كالإذن من الثيبات ليعتبر توكيلاً يسري به عليهن عقد التزويج منذ وقوعه من الولي المستأذن. وعند عدم الإذن بالتوكيل من البكر البالغة يعتبر تزويجها عملاً فضولياً يحتاج إلى إجازة منها ليصبح نافذاً عليها، وإن سكتت عند استثمار وليها لها .
الفتاة
8/71 - This, and among the specific legislative texts that are considered—in my view—built upon custom and habit, so their ruling changes according to the change of habit, is what we have previously mentioned of the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him, regarding the mature virgin girl: 'Her consent is her silence.' (Reference: F 9/69b) where her silence when her guardian asks her permission to marry her to a specific man with a specific dowry is considered as consent and authorization (Reference: F 7/3 and 10/69). The jurists have agreed that this ruling regarding the virgin girl is based on what is known of her shyness to show her desire for marriage when her guardian consults her. Her custom is to express her desire and consent by silence. This shyness is still dominant among them in most Islamic circles according to upbringing and social traditions. If we suppose that this upbringing changes direction and the virgin girls become unabashed about declaring this desire or its absence equally (like the custom of non-virgin women), then their consent to marriage at that time is not sufficient by silence; rather, at that time a statement is needed like the consent of non-virgins so that it is considered authorization that makes the marriage contract binding upon them from the moment it is concluded by the guardian who sought permission. And in the absence of authorization from the mature virgin, her marriage is considered a gratuitous act (fuduli) that requires her ratification to become effective, even if she remains silent when her guardian consults her.
Source pages p. 853

Commentary
This passage provides another example of a ruling that is based on custom ('urf or 'adah) and thus subject to change when the custom changes. The ruling is the well-known hadith that a virgin's consent is her silence (idhnuha samatuha). The author explains that this ruling is built on the customary shyness of virgin girls, which prevents them from openly expressing their desire for marriage. Jurists agree on this reasoning. However, if social upbringing changes such that virgin girls are no longer shy and are like non-virgins (thayyibat) in openly expressing their wishes, then silence can no longer be considered consent. In that case, the virgin must explicitly state her consent, just like a non-virgin. If she remains silent, the marriage contract is considered fuduli (unauthorized) and requires her later ratification to become effective. This passage illustrates the Hanafi principle that rulings based on custom evolve with custom, even if they originate from a specific hadith. The author (Mustafa al-Zarqa) uses this to show that not all nusus are immutable; some are intrinsically linked to custom and change accordingly.

Key Terms
إذنها صماتها (idhnuha samatuha): The prophetic saying that a virgin's consent is her silence; silence is deemed as permission.
بكر (bikr): Virgin: a female who has never been married.
فضولي (fuduli): Unauthorized agent: a person who acts on behalf of another without permission; the contract is suspended until ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the custom of shyness disappears among virgin girls, what becomes of the ruling that silence equals consent?
A) The ruling remains unchanged because it is from the Prophet.
B) Silence still suffices, but only for mature virgins.
C) Explicit consent is required, and silence alone is insufficient.
D) The ruling becomes void and no marriage can be contracted.

Key Takeaway
Rulings based on custom, even those rooted in prophetic hadith, adapt when the underlying custom changes.

Interpreting General Texts with Concurrent Verbal Custom
Rule that concurrent verbal custom takes precedence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a concurrent verbal custom takes precedence over the literal meaning of a general text when issuing fatwas.

Core Idea
Verbal custom concurrent with revelation governs interpretation of general texts.

When a general legislative text was revealed, the community may have already had a customary understanding of certain words. This lesson explains that if that custom is verbal and existed at the time of revelation, the text is interpreted according to that custom, unless there is evidence that the Lawgiver intended otherwise. This is a key principle for muftis in deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish concurrent vs. subsequent custom
Identify whether the custom existed at the time of the text's revelation (concurrent) or arose later. Only concurrent custom is relevant to this rule.
Subsequent custom is treated in the next chapter.
2
Classify the custom as verbal or practical
Determine if the custom concerns the meaning of words (verbal) or practices. This rule applies only to verbal custom.
Practical custom has different considerations.
3
Apply the priority rule
If the custom is verbal and concurrent, interpret the general text within the limits of its customary meaning, even if the literal meaning is broader.
This is based on unanimous agreement of jurists.
4
Check for evidence of broader intent
If there is contextual evidence (qarina) indicating that the Lawgiver intended a broader meaning than the custom, then the custom is overridden.
Absence of such evidence is presumed.

الفصل الثاني والسبعون
العرف المقارن لورود النص العام المعارض له
١/٧٢ - إذا عارض العرفُ نصاً تشريعياً عاماً شاملاً بعمومه الأمر المتعارف(۱)، ففي سلطان العرف عندئذ تفصيل بحسب كون العرف مقارناً لورود النص وهذا ما نبحثه في هذا الفصل أو حادثاً بعده (وهو موضوع الفصل / ٧٣ التالي).
۲/۷۲ - العرف القائم عند ورود النص التشريعي إما أن يكون عرفاً لفظياً أو عملياً :
-
فإذا كان لفظياً فلا خلاف بين الفقهاء في اعتباره، فينزل النص التشريعي العام على حدود معناه العرفي عند الخلو عن القرائن، ولو كانت دلالة اللفظ الذي استعمله الشارع هي في أصل اللغة أوسع من دلالته العرفية، لأن العرف اللفظي العام هو لغة التخاطب، فيوجه الألفاظ إلى معانيها في حدودها المألوفة ما لم توجد قرينة دالة على أن الشارع أراد بلفظه حدوداً أوسع.
وهذا في الواقع فرع من قاعدة وجوب حمل اللفظ على معناه
(۱) سمينا الحالة السابقة في الفصل / ۷۱ اصطداماً بين العرف والنص لأن النص إذا كان خاصاً فكأنما يصادمه العرف المخالف وجهاً لوجه .
أما هذه الحالة هنا فنسميها تعارضاً بين العرف والنص لأن النص عندما يكون عاماً يكون موضوع العرف المخالف هو بعض مشمولاته ولم يرد النص بشأنه خصيصاً، فكأنما يعترض العرف المخالف اعتراضاً في بعض اتجاهات عموم النص، كخطين هندسيين يتقاطعان في نقطة.
Chapter 72: Concurrent custom with the occurrence of the general text opposing it. 1/72 - If custom opposes a general legislative text that includes in its generality the customary matter(1), then the authority of custom in that case has detail depending on whether the custom is concurrent with the occurrence of the text—this is what we discuss in this chapter—or occurs after it (which is the subject of the next chapter/73). 2/72 - The custom existing at the time of the legislative text's occurrence is either verbal or practical: If it is verbal, then there is no disagreement among the jurists regarding its consideration. The general legislative text is interpreted within the limits of its customary meaning when there are no contextual indications, even if the linguistic meaning of the term used by the Lawgiver is broader than its customary indication, because the general verbal custom is the language of communication; it directs words to their meanings within their familiar limits unless there is an indication that the Lawgiver intended broader limits for His word. This is in fact a branch of the rule requiring that a word be interpreted according to its meaning. (1) We called the previous case in Chapter 71 a collision between custom and text because if the text is specific, it is as if the opposing custom confronts it face to face. As for this case here, we call it a conflict between custom and text because when the text is general, the subject of the opposing custom is some of its inclusions, and the text did not specifically address it; it is as if the opposing custom objects in some directions of the text's generality, like two geometric lines intersecting at a point.
Source pages p. 855

Commentary
This passage introduces a key methodological rule in usul al-iftaa: how to handle a general legislative text when a custom exists that is concurrent with its revelation. The passage distinguishes between verbal custom (urf lafdhi) and practical custom (urf amali). It states that if the custom is verbal, there is unanimous agreement among jurists that the text is interpreted according to that customary meaning, not the broader literal meaning, unless there is evidence that the Lawgiver intended a different meaning. This is because verbal custom is the language of address. The passage also clarifies the terminology: when the text is specific, the opposition is called 'collision' (sadama); when the text is general, it is called 'conflict' (ta'arud), because the custom only opposes part of the general text's scope. This is a fundamental rule for muftis: when issuing fatwas, they must consider the customary understanding of terms at the time of the text's revelation, unless evidence suggests otherwise.

Key Terms
عرف لفظي (urf lafdhi): Verbal custom: the customary usage of words in a particular community.
نص عام (nass aam): A general legislative text that includes many particulars under its generality.
دلالة عرفية (dalalah urfiyyah): Customary indication: the meaning given to a word by common usage, which may be narrower than its original linguistic meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling when a concurrent verbal custom opposes a general legislative text, according to the passage?
A) The text overrides the custom regardless.
B) The custom is considered, and the text is interpreted within its customary limits.
C) There is disagreement among jurists about considering it.
D) The custom is only considered if it is practical, not verbal.

Key Takeaway
Concurrent verbal custom governs the interpretation of general legislative texts unless evidence indicates the Lawgiver intended a broader meaning.

Contemporaneous Custom and Its Role in Specifying General Texts
Explains the rule for when existing custom countermands a general legal text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a general practical custom contemporaneous to a text can specify its general application, and identify the distinction between verbal and practical custom.

Core Idea
A general custom at revelation can specify a text; verbal custom always controls meaning.

The interaction between custom ('urf) and general legislative texts (nusus 'ammah) is a critical area in Hanafi usul al-iftaa. This passage introduces the foundational rule: when a custom existed at the time of revelation, it can shape the scope of a general text. Understanding this rule is essential for muftis who must determine whether a prevailing practice falls under a general prohibition or permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of custom
Distinguish between verbal custom (al-'urf al-lafzi) and practical custom (al-'urf al-'amali). The text states that verbal custom is universally accepted; practical custom is subject to debate.
Verbal custom relates to word meanings; practical custom relates to actions.
2
Apply the rule for verbal custom
When a general text uses a word, it is interpreted according to its customary meaning, even if the linguistic meaning is broader. This is because customary meaning becomes the true meaning (haqiqah 'urfiya).
Example: 'sale' (al-bay') includes deferred payment sales if customary.
3
Recognize the dispute on practical custom
Some jurists do not accept practical custom as a specifier. The Hanafi school does, but only if the custom is general and contemporaneous.
The opposing view is that only textual evidence can specify a general text.
4
Apply the Hanafi condition: general and contemporaneous
For practical custom to specify, it must be general (common among all people) and existing at the time of revelation. The custom acts as a qarinah that the Lawgiver did not intend the general ruling to cover that practice.
If the custom is specific to a region, it does not specify.
5
Derive the fatwa consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is that a Hanafi mufti may permit a customary transaction that a literal reading of the text would prohibit, based on the specifying custom.
For example, 'urbun (earnest money) sale is sometimes permitted based on custom.

الفصل الثاني والسبعون
العرف المقارن لورود النص العام المعارض له
١/٧٢ - إذا عارض العرفُ نصاً تشريعياً عاماً شاملاً بعمومه الأمر المتعارف(١)، ففي سلطان العرف عندئذ تفصيل بحسب كون العرف مقارناً لورود النص وهذا ما نبحثه في هذا الفصل أو حادثاً بعده (وهو موضوع الفصل / ٧٣ التالي).
۲/۷۲ - العرف القائم عند ورود النص التشريعي إما أن يكون عرفاً لفظياً أو عملياً :
-
فإذا كان لفظياً فلا خلاف بين الفقهاء في اعتباره، فينزل النص التشريعي العام على حدود معناه العرفي عند الخلو عن القرائن، ولو كانت دلالة اللفظ الذي استعمله الشارع هي في أصل اللغة أوسع من دلالته العرفية، لأن العرف اللفظي العام هو لغة التخاطب، فيوجه الألفاظ إلى معانيها في حدودها المألوفة ما لم توجد قرينة دالة على أن الشارع أراد بلفظه حدوداً أوسع.
وهذا في الواقع فرع من قاعدة وجوب حمل اللفظ على معناه الحقيقي ما لم تقم قرينة على إرادة المجاز، لأن العرف اللفظي يجعل المعنى المتعارف حقيقة عرفية وهي مقدّمة في الفهم على الحقيقة اللغوية التي تصبح هي بالنسبة إلى الحقيقة العرفية، مجازاً يحتاج إلى قرينة . فألفاظ البيع والشراء والإجارة والصيام والصلاة والحج، وعدة النساء الطلاق والوفاة، كل ذلك ونحوه في النصوص يحمل على معانيه العرفية عند ورود النصوص بها، وإن اختلفت عن المعاني الوضعية في أصل اللغة .
:
ب - وأما إذا كان العرف القائم عند ورود النص المخالف له عرفاً عملياً فقد اختلفت الاجتهادات في كونه صالحاً لتخصيص النص العام أو غير صالح.
ولدى من يعتبره صالحاً يوجد تفصيل بين أن يكون العرف عاماً أو خاصاً، نلخصه فيما يلي:
٣/٧٢ - أ - العرف المقارن العام :
فإذا كان العرف القائم عند ورود النص العام المعارض له عرفاً عاماً فإنه في الاجتهاد الحنفي يكون مخصصاً للنص، فيعتبر عندئذ ذلك النص مقصور الشمول على ما سوى الأمر المتعارف إذ يكون ذلك العرف قرينة قائمة دالة على أنَّ الشارع لم يرد شمول ذلك الأمر المتعارف بعموم نصه المعارض له في الظاهر حكمه.
(١) التخصيص في اصطلاح علم أصول الفقه هو : قصر اللفظ العام على بعض أفراده. وذلك بأن يرد عن الشارع نص يتضمن حكماً عاماً ويقوم دليل آخر يفيد أن الشارع لم يرد بذلك النص العام أن يتناول حكمه كل ما يدخل في عموم لفظه، بل هناك حالة أو حالات مستثناة من وهذا الدليل المخصص يكون إما نصاً آخر مقارناً للأول يستثني منه بعض مشمولاته، أو قرينة قائمة تدل على هذا التخصيص، وتفيد أن الشارع قد عبر باللفظ العام غير مريد شموله لهذه الحالات التي قام الدليل على استثنائها منه .
مثال ذلك قوله تعالى: (وَأَحَلَ اللَّهُ الْبَيْعَ وَحَرَّمَ الرِّبوا ) :
Chapter Seventy-Two: The Custom Contemporary to the Revelation of a Contrary General Text. 1/72 - If custom opposes a general legislative text that includes in its generality a conventional matter, then the authority of custom at that time has a detail depending on whether the custom is contemporary to the revelation of the text – this is what we study in this chapter – or occurs after it (which is the subject of the following chapter 73). 2/72 - The custom existing at the revelation of the legislative text is either verbal or practical. If it is verbal, there is no disagreement among jurists in considering it; the general legislative text is interpreted within the limits of its customary meaning in the absence of contextual indicators, even if the lexical meaning of the word used by the Lawgiver is broader than its customary meaning, because general verbal custom is the language of address, so it directs words to their meanings within familiar limits unless there is an indicator that the Lawgiver intended with His word broader limits. This is in reality a branch of the rule that a word must be interpreted in its literal meaning unless there is evidence of figurative intent, because verbal custom makes the conventional meaning a customary reality, which takes precedence in understanding over the lexical reality, which becomes, relative to the customary reality, a figurative meaning needing an indicator. Thus, the words 'sale', 'purchase', 'lease', 'fasting', 'prayer', 'pilgrimage', the waiting period of women (due to divorce or death), and all such things in the texts are interpreted according to their customary meanings when the texts are revealed with them, even if they differ from the original lexical meanings. B - If the custom existing at the revelation of the opposing text is a practical custom, then ijtihads have differed on whether it is valid to specify the general text or not. And among those who consider it valid, there is a distinction between whether the custom is general or specific; we summarize it as follows: 3/72 - A - The general contemporary custom: If the custom existing at the revelation of the contrary general text is a general custom, then in Hanafi ijtihad it serves as a specifier of the text; so that text is considered limited in scope to what is other than the conventional matter, because that custom is an existing indicator that the Lawgiver did not intend to include that conventional matter in the generality of His apparently contrary text. (1) 'Specification' in the terminology of usul al-fiqh is: restricting a general word to some of its individual instances. This occurs when the Lawgiver reveals a text containing a general ruling, and another evidence arises indicating that the Lawgiver did not intend by that general text that its ruling should encompass everything included in the generality of its word, but rather there is a case or cases excepted. And this specifying evidence is either another text contemporary to the first that excepts some of its constituents, or an existing indicator that indicates this specification, and informs that the Lawgiver expressed with the general word not intending its inclusion of these cases for which evidence establishes their exception. Example of that is His saying: 'And Allah has permitted sale and forbidden usury.'
Source pages p. 855

Commentary
This passage is a rule from Hanafi usul al-iftaa regarding the interaction between custom ('urf) and general legislative texts (nusus 'ammah). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, divides the discussion into two scenarios based on when the custom arises relative to the text. Here he treats the first scenario: custom contemporary to the revelation of the text. He further distinguishes between verbal custom and practical custom. For verbal custom (e.g., how terms like 'sale' or 'prayer' are understood in common usage), there is complete consensus: the general text is interpreted according to that customary meaning, because the customary understanding takes precedence over the original linguistic meaning unless there is a clear indicator that the Lawgiver intended the broader linguistic sense. This is a branch of the general hermeneutic rule that words are taken in their literal (haqiqi) sense unless there is evidence of figurative (majaz) usage. For practical custom, the Hanafi position is that if the custom is general (i.e., prevailing among all people) and was in place at the time of revelation, it can specify the general text, meaning the general ruling is considered not to apply to that customary practice. This is because the custom serves as a qarinah (contextual indicator) that the Lawgiver did not intend to include that practice. Other schools may disagree, but the passage notes that among those who accept practical custom as a specifier, there is further detail between general and specific custom. The footnote provides a standard definition of 'takhsis' (specification) from usul al-fiqh, essential for understanding this rule. The rule has practical implications: for example, the general Qur'anic permission of 'sale' (al-bay') is taken in its customary sense, and certain customary sale transactions that were practiced at the time of revelation (like 'urbun or deferred payment sales) are included or excluded based on the custom's interpretation.

Key Terms
العرف المقارن (al-‘urf al-muqārin): The custom that existed at the time of the revelation of the legal text.
العرف اللفظي (al-‘urf al-lafẓī): Verbal custom: the conventional understanding of words in common usage.
العرف العملي (al-‘urf al-‘amalī): Practical custom: established practice or behavior among people.
التخصيص (al-takhṣīṣ): Restricting a general word to some of its individuals by evidence.
العموم (al-‘umūm): Generality of a word that encompasses all its individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi stance presented in the text, when can a practical custom that existed at the time of revelation specify a general text?
A) If the custom is general (common among all people).
B) If the custom is verbal (linguistic).
C) If the custom is specific to a particular region.
D) It never can specify a general text.

Key Takeaway
A general practical custom contemporaneous with a text can specify its general application in the Hanafi school, acting as a qarinah of legislative intent.

Specific Custom Cannot Specify General Texts: Hanafi & Maliki Views
Discusses the Hanafi rule that specific custom cannot specify general texts, with Maliki comparison.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that specific custom does not specify general texts, apply it to a fatwa scenario, and evaluate the Maliki counter-position.

Core Idea
A specific custom cannot specify a general text because specification explains the Lawgiver's intent, and doubt cannot establish it.

This lesson examines a critical rule in Hanafi fatwa methodology: specific custom ('urf khāṣṣ) cannot specify a general legislative text. Understanding this distinction prevents incorrect fatwas that might arise from applying local or limited customs to override clear scriptural commands. The passage also presents the Maliki school's more flexible approach and a classic example from Imam Malik regarding breastfeeding obligations.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the Rule
Extract the precise Hanafi rule: a specific custom (confined to a place or group) does not suffice to specify a general text.
Contrast with general custom which may specify according to some later Hanafi opinions.
2
2. Understand the Talil (Rationale)
The reason: specification (takhṣīṣ) is an explanation of the Lawgiver's original intent, not a subsequent change. If the custom is not widespread, we cannot be certain the Lawgiver intended it to apply universally; doubt cannot establish specification.
This talil is rooted in the Hanafi epistemological requirement of certainty (yaqīn) for overriding general texts.
3
3. Compare with the Maliki Position
Note that most Maliki jurists accept operative custom (even if specific) as a valid specifier, provided it does not contradict Sharia. Their approach is more flexible, based on practice of Madinah and maṣlaḥah.
The passage mentions that the Malikis do not explicitly distinguish between general and specific custom.
4
4. Apply to a Fatwa Scenario
Consider how a mufti would apply this rule. For example, a local custom among some merchants to delay payment cannot override the general Quranic command to fulfill contracts promptly.
This illustrates the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf) between the schools.

Source Text

الفصل (72) العرف المقارن لنص عام يعارضه
لأنه إذا كان عرف بعض البلاد أو الناس يقتضي تخصيص النص، فإن عدم هذا العرف لدى بقية الأماكن أو الناس لا يقتضيه فلا يثبت التخصيص بالشك (1).
(1) وقد رأينا آنفاً أن تخصيص النص التشريعي العام هو بيان لمراد الشارع منه لا تعديل طارىء عليه (ر) ف (3/72 الحاشية، والعرف الخاص قد يكون بين فئة ضئيلة من الناس أو في مكان صغير، فلا يصلح دليلاً على أن الشارع لم يرد من نصه العام عموم معناه.
5/72 - هذا تحرير ما في الاجتهاد الحنفي في هذا المقام.
وفي المذهب المالكي اختلاف بين فقهائه حول اعتبار العرف العملي مخصصاً لعموم النص. ولكن المحققين من فقهاء المذهب على أنَّ العرف العملي القائم يخصص النص العام كما يقيد النصَّ المطلق خلافاً لما قاله القرافي في فروقه (173/1 الفرق / 28) من أن العرف العملي لا يصلح لتخصيص النص أو تقييده.
كذلك لا يصرحون بالتمييز بين أن يكون العرف عاماً أو خاصاً. وهم
ر حاشية شمس الدين الدسوقي على الشرح الكبير 143/2)(2). ونجد من فروع هذا المبدأ عند الإمام مالك أنه يقول إذا كانت المرأة شريفة القدر لا يلزمها إرضاع ولدها إن كان يقبل ثدي غيرها للمصلحة العرفية في ذلك حيث جرى العرف لدى أمثالهن على أن تستأجر لأولادهن
(1) الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجيم آخر القاعدة السادسة 134/1، ورسالة «نشر العرف) لابن عابدين الباب الأول /116/2 من مجموعة رسائله.
(2) أفاض في تحقيق مذهب الحنفية والمالكية وغيرهما من مسألة تخصيص تخصيص النص التشريعي العام بالعرف العملي الأستاذ الفاضل أحمد فهمي أبو سنة في المقالين الخامس والسابع من رسالته العرف والعادة ص 91 - 94 و 124.

الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
المراضع. قال ابن العربي: إن هذا تخصيص لعموم قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالْوَالِدَاتُ يُرْضِعْنَ أَولَدَهُنَّ حَوْلَيْنِ كَامِلَيْنِ﴾ (1).
(ر) أحكام القرآن لابن العربي تحت الآية 323 من سورة البقرة).
(1) يعد بعض المالكية هذه المسألة من فروع تخصيص النص بالمصلحة، ولكن المصلحة التي يقصدونها هنا إنما هي مراعاة العرف الجاري في صَوْن النساء ذوات الشرف والنبل عن الإرضاع وتربية أولادهن بطريق الاسترضاع وهو من الأعراف التي لا تنافي قواعد الشريعة ونصوصها فتكون رعايته من المصالح، ويخصص به النص الشرعي . وقد كانت عادة العرب النبلاء في الجاهلية أن يسترضعوا لأولادهم مرضعات في البادية ابتغاء صحة الأجسام وتقويم اللسان.
Chapter (72): The Specific Custom Opposing a General Text. Because if the custom of some lands or people necessitates specification of the text, then the absence of this custom in other places or among other people does not necessitate it, so specification is not established by doubt (1). (1) And we saw earlier that specification of a general legislative text is an explanation of the intent of the Lawgiver from it, not an incidental amendment. And the specific custom may be among a small group of people or in a small place, so it is not valid as evidence that the Lawgiver did not intend the general meaning of His general text. 5/72 - This is the verification of what is in Hanafi ijtihad in this regard. In the Maliki school, there is disagreement among its jurists regarding considering operative custom as specifying the generality of the text. But the verified jurists of the school hold that operative custom specifies the general text just as it restricts the absolute text, contrary to what al-Qarafi said in his Furūq (1/173, al-Farq 28) that operative custom is not valid for specifying or restricting a text. Likewise, they do not explicitly distinguish between whether the custom is general or specific. And we find from the branches of this principle with Imam Malik that he says: If a woman is of noble status, she is not obliged to breastfeed her child if it accepts another's breast, due to the customary interest in that, where the custom among her peers is to hire wet nurses for their children. (1) Al-Ashbāh wa al-Naẓā'ir by Ibn Nujaym, end of the sixth principle, 1/134, and the treatise Nashr al-Urf by Ibn Abidin, first chapter, 2/116 of his collection of treatises. (2) The distinguished professor Ahmad Fahmi Abu Sunnah has discussed in detail the Hanafi and Maliki schools and others regarding the issue of specifying general legislative text by operative custom in the fifth and seventh articles of his treatise al-Urf wa al-Ādah, pp. 91-94 and 124. Ninth Chapter: Theory of Custom. Wet nurses. Ibn al-Arabi said: This is a specification of the generality of His statement: "Mothers shall breastfeed their children for two complete years" (2:233). (Reference: Ahkām al-Qur'ān by Ibn al-Arabi under the verse 323 of Surah al-Baqarah). (1) Some Malikis consider this issue as a branch of specifying the text by interest (maslahah), but the interest intended here is only observance of the prevailing custom of protecting women of honor and nobility from breastfeeding and raising their children by way of hiring wet nurses, and this is among the customs that do not contradict the rules and texts of Sharia; so observing it is among the interests, and the legal text is specified by it. The noble Arabs in pre-Islamic times used to hire wet nurses for their children in the desert seeking physical health and correcting the tongue.
Source pages p. 859

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi rasm rule regarding the interaction between specific custom ('urf khāṣṣ) and general legislative texts (nuṣūṣ ʿāmmah). The rule is that a custom confined to a particular land or segment of people cannot be used to specify the general meaning of a text, because specification (takhṣīṣ) is an explanation of the Lawgiver's intent, not a modification. If the custom is not widespread, there is no evidence that the Lawgiver intended that specification. The passage clarifies that this is the verified position of the Hanafi school. It then presents the Maliki school's stance, noting disagreement: most Maliki jurists accept operative custom as a valid specifier of general texts and restrictor of absolute texts, contrary to al-Qarafi's isolated view. The Malikis do not explicitly differentiate between general and specific custom. An example from Imam Malik is given: a noble woman is not obliged to breastfeed her child if it accepts another's breast, due to the prevailing custom among her peers of hiring wet nurses. This is considered a specification of the general Quranic command to breastfeed by custom and interest (maṣlaḥah). The passage concludes by noting that this custom does not contradict Sharia principles.

Key Terms
العرف الخاص (al-‘urf al-khāṣṣ): A custom that is confined to a particular place or a specific group of people, not widespread.
تخصيص النص العام (takhṣīṣ al-naṣṣ al-‘āmm): Excluding some individuals or cases from the general meaning of a legislative text, based on evidence that the Lawgiver did not intend absolute generality.
النص العام (al-naṣṣ al-‘āmm): A text whose wording encompasses all individuals falling under its scope without limitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, why does a specific custom (al-‘urf al-khāṣṣ) not suffice to specify a general legislative text?
A) Because custom is never considered evidence in Hanafi fiqh.
B) Because specification is an explanation of the Lawgiver's intent, and a specific custom does not prove that intent for places where the custom is absent.
C) Because only explicit scriptural evidence can specify texts, not custom.
D) Because the Hanafi school does not recognize the concept of custom at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule that 'specific custom does not specify general texts' is rooted in the principle that specification explains the Lawgiver's intent, and only general custom provides sufficient certainty.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
6 min
The Rule for Custom Arising After a General Text
Establishes the rule for when a new custom contradicts a general text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a later custom cannot specify a general text, and identify the exception when the text's underlying cause is removed.

Core Idea
Later custom cannot override a general text unless it removes the text's underlying cause.

When a custom arises after a general legal text, can it change the meaning of that text? The Hanafi school has a clear principle: only the custom that existed at the time of the text can specify it. A later custom has no such authority, unless the text itself was based on a custom that has now changed, or its underlying cause has been removed by the new custom. This lesson unpacks that rule and its application through the classic example of the word 'ustadh'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal conflict
Recognize that a later custom ('urf tari') contradicts a general text (nass 'amm). The text may be a Quranic verse, hadith, or a juristic ruling that uses general words.
The conflict is not about abrogation but about interpretation and specification.
2
State the default Hanafi rule
Memorize the principle: 'Custom upon which words are based is only the concurrent preceding custom, not the later one; therefore, no regard is given to a later custom.' This means the general text prevails over the later custom.
This rule applies to both verbal and practical customs, as the text states.
3
Recognize the exception
Identify when a later custom is considered: if the text itself is based on a custom (mua'llal bi al-urf) or its underlying cause (illa) is removed by the new custom. Then the later custom can override the text.
The exception is captured in the sub-maxim (dabt): 'unless the text is based on custom or based on a cause that the custom removes.'
4
Apply the rule to the 'ustadh' example
Analyze the example: The classical fiqh ruling about wages between a master craftsman (ustadh) and a father assumed a custom where the master benefits from the apprentice's work. Modern custom changed 'ustadh' to mean teacher, who does not benefit from the student. Thus, the old ruling does not apply.
The later custom (teacher) does not specify the earlier general text because it does not remove the underlying cause (benefit from work).
5
Distinguish between general and specific custom
Note that only a general custom (al-urf al-amm) coexisting with the text can specify it. A specific custom (al-urf al-khass) does not have such authority except on a weak opinion.
This distinction is important for fatwa: a mufti must assess whether the custom is general or specific.

فينتفع فيها من عمل الولد ولذا يختلف عرف القوم في أخذ الأجرة وإعطائها بحسب حاجة الصناعة إلى الأجير ورغبة الناس في تعلمها، واشتمالها على أسرار لا يفهمها الصانع إلا بالتفهيم والتدريب الخاص. وهذا لا يتأتى في الأستاذ المدرس للعلوم، فإنَّ التدريس خدمة أبداً من المعلم للمتعلم .

العرف الذي تحمل عليه الألفاظ إنما هو المقارن السابق دون المتأخر، ولذا قالوا لا عبرة بالعرف الطارىء».

Chapter (73): Custom arising after a general text that contradicts it. (1) ... (ref). 2/73 - On this basis, the texts of jurists must also be applied, and endowment deeds, wills, sales, gifts, marriages, and all contractual documents and the conditions and terms therein must be interpreted according to the custom of the contracting parties at their time, by analogy to the texts of the Lawgiver, and it is not permissible to interpret them according to a later custom that would take their speech out of their intended meaning. For example, the word 'ustadh' originally meant, by custom, the master of a craft who employs an apprentice to teach him the craft. Based on this custom, the jurists ruled: If a person sends his young son to a master (ustadh) and they later dispute over entitlement to wages, the master demanding wages from the father for the son, and the father demanding wages from the master for him, who is entitled to wages from the other? They said: recourse is made to the custom of the town; if the custom of the town is that the master takes wages or pays wages, that custom is followed. (Ref: Majalla 569, al-Qawa'id al-Fiqhiyya by al-Hamzawi in matters of ijarah p. 76). In our time, the word 'ustadh' has come to mean teacher of sciences, so it is not correct to apply that fiqh text to this new meaning, because that detail does not apply, since the master of the craft teaches the boy by employing him in the craft, benefiting from his work. ... (1) Divorce and manumission are matters in which jest is considered like earnest. (Ref: F 6/33). Page 864: Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. So they benefit from the boy's work, and thus the custom of the people regarding taking and giving wages varies according to the need of the craft for workers and people's desire to learn it, and its inclusion of secrets that the craftsman only understands through special teaching and training. This does not apply to the teacher of sciences, for teaching is always a service from teacher to student. What he said: Ibn Nujaym said in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir under the sixth maxim, 1/133: 'Custom upon which words are based is only the concurrent preceding custom, not the later one; therefore, they said: no regard is given to a later custom.' You know that disregarding later custom in specifying the meanings of texts and restricting them is not limited to verbal custom; later practical custom also does not have this authority over the text, even if it is a general custom, unless the text is based on custom or based on a cause that the custom removes, as we will soon see (F 4/73). 3/73 - This is the established fiqh principle when custom and a general legislative text conflict, summarized as: a) If the custom existed when the text came, then general custom in Hanafi ijtihad has authority to specify the text and restrict its ruling to matters other than the customary practice; specific custom does not have such authority except according to a weak and outweighed opinion. b) If the custom arises later, it cannot specify the earlier general text; rather, the general text prevails over the custom, even if the custom is general. 4/73 - Custom that removes the cause of a conflicting text is considered, even if it is later. However, tracing fiqh branches and examining their rationales... (page 922 continued).
Source pages p. 863

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental principle of Hanafi legal theory regarding the interaction between custom (urf) and general legislative texts (nusus ammah). The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, divides the discussion into two main scenarios. First, if the custom existed at the time the text was revealed (or issued), then in Hanafi ijtihad, a general custom can specify (takhsis) the general text, meaning the text's ruling is confined to non-customary matters. A specific (private) custom does not have this power except on a weak opinion. Second, if the custom arises after the text, it cannot specify that earlier text; the generality of the text prevails, even if the custom becomes widespread. However, there is an important exception: if the text itself is based on a custom, or its underlying cause (illa) is something that the new custom removes, then the new custom is considered, even if it arises later. The passage illustrates this with the example of the word 'ustadh', which originally meant a master craftsman taking an apprentice, but later came to mean a teacher of sciences. The classical fiqh ruling about wages between master and father cannot be applied to the modern teacher because the underlying circumstances differ. The principle is quoted from Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir. This is a rasm rule for muftis: when issuing fatwas, one must be aware of the temporal relationship between custom and text, and only give specifying power to customs that existed at the time of the text, unless the text's cause is affected.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): A recurring practice accepted by people of sound nature; a source of law in the absence of a text, and a specifying factor for general texts when coexistent.
العرف الطارىء (al-urf al-tari'): A custom that arises after the existence of a legal text; generally not given power to specify that text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle discussed, when does a later custom ('urf tari') have authority over a general text?
A) Always, if the custom is widespread among the people.
B) When the custom is verbal rather than practical.
C) When the custom removes the cause (illa) on which the text is based.
D) When the custom is endorsed by a later jurist.

Key Takeaway
Later custom cannot specify a general text unless it removes the text's underlying cause.

Custom Overrides Theoretical Qiyas in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the rule that custom prevails over qiyas and istislah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that custom prevails over theoretical qiyas in issuing fatwas, and identify when custom can override derived rulings without contradicting a textual nass.

Core Idea
Custom overrides theoretical qiyas because custom indicates need and is stronger than qiyas in the absence of text.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa presents a foundational principle for muftis: when a ruling is derived through theoretical qiyas (analogy) or istislah (public interest) and a relevant custom exists that does not contradict a direct text, the custom takes precedence. This rule is rooted in the Hanafi concept of istihsan and reflects the school's recognition of the evolving nature of social practices. Understanding when custom can override derived rulings is essential for issuing sound fatwas in changing contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of ijtihad under consideration
The passage distinguishes between rulings derived from theoretical qiyas (based on an inferred cause from a text) and those from istihsan or istislah (based on juristic preference or public interest). Note which types are subject to being overridden by custom.
Theoretical qiyas refers to analogy where the effective cause is inferred, not explicitly stated.
2
State the rule and its condition
The rule: custom overrides the qiyas-derived ruling. The condition: the custom must not directly contradict a specific or general textual ruling (nass). Memorize the exact wording from the passage.
The passage says 'العرف لا يعارضه نص خاص ولا عام معارضة مباشرة'.
3
Understand the rationale
Custom is considered evidence of need (dalil al-haja), and need-based reasoning is stronger than theoretical analogy. Also, Ibn al-Humam equates custom's authority to that of consensus in the absence of text.
The rationale is based on the principle that need (haja) can elevate custom to a quasi-obligatory status.
4
Extend the rule to istislah
The author argues that if custom overrides qiyas (which has some link to text), it certainly overrides istislah (which is based solely on temporal interest and is more changeable).
This extension shows the hierarchy: custom > qiyas > istislah when all are without direct textual support.

الفصل الرابع والسبعون
حالة تعارض العرف والاجتهاد
١/٧٤ - أولاً - في الاجتهاد المستند إلى قياس نظري واستحسان أو
استصلاح الأحكام الاجتهادية التي يثبتها الفقهاء المجتهدون استنباطاً وتخريجاً عند عدم النص الشرعي عليها، إما أن تكون ثابتة بطريق القياس النظري على حكم أوجبه الشارع بالنص لاتحاد العلة بين الحكم المقيس عليه والمقيس، وإما أن تكون ثابتة بطريق الاستحسان أو الاستصلاح عندما لا يوجد حكم مشابه منصوص يقاس عليه.
والاجتهادات الإسلامية تكاد تكون متفقة على أن الحكم القياسي يترك للعرف ولو كان عرفاً حادثاً، لأنَّ المفروض عندئذ أن هذا العرف لا يعارضه نص خاص ولا عام معارضة مباشرة والعرف غالباً دليل الحاجة، فهو أقوى من القياس، فيترجح عليه عند التعارض (ر: نشر العرف لابن عابدين في مجموعة رسائله ٢/ ١١٦).
·
بل لقد ذكر العلامة المحقق ابن الهمام في شرح الهداية أن العرف بمنزلة الإجماع شرعاً عند عدم النص» (ر: فتح القدير ١٥٧/٦).
ومن المعلوم أن ترجيح العرف على القياس يعتبر عند الحنفية والمالكية من قبيل الاستحسان الذي تترك فيه الدلائل القياسية لأدلة أخرى منها العرف.
وإذا كان العرف يترجح على القياس الذي يستند إلى نص تشريعي غير مباشر، فهذا يدل على أنه يترجح أيضاً على الاستصلاح الذي لا يستند إلى نص، بل إلى مجرد المصلحة الزمنية التي هي عرضة للتبدل بحسب اختلاف الأزمنة وما يجد فيها من أوضاع ومقتضيات.
Chapter Seventy-Four: The Case of Conflict Between Custom and Ijtihad. 1/74 - Firstly: Regarding ijtihad based on theoretical qiyas and istihsan or istislah. The ijtihadi rulings that the mujtahid jurists establish by deduction and derivation in the absence of a textual ruling are either established by way of theoretical qiyas on a ruling that the Lawgiver obligated by text due to the unity of the cause between the analogized ruling and the original, or they are established by way of istihsan or istislah when there is no similar textually prescribed ruling to analogize to. Islamic ijtihads are almost unanimous that the qiyasi ruling is left for custom even if it is a newly arisen custom, because the assumption then is that this custom is not directly contradicted by a specific or general text, and custom is often evidence of need, so it is stronger than qiyas, thus it prevails over it in case of conflict (see: Nashr al-Urf by Ibn Abidin in his collected treatises 2/116). Indeed, the verifying scholar Ibn al-Humam mentioned in Sharh al-Hidaya that 'custom is in the position of consensus legally in the absence of a text' (see: Fath al-Qadir 6/157). It is well known that giving preference to custom over qiyas is considered by the Hanafis and Malikis as a type of istihsan in which qiyasic evidences are abandoned for other evidences including custom. If custom prevails over qiyas that is based on an indirect textual legislation, then this indicates that it also prevails over istislah that is not based on a text but merely on temporal interest which is subject to change according to the difference of times and the conditions and necessities that arise in them.
Source pages p. 872

Commentary
This passage presents a key principle of Hanafi usul al-iftaa: when custom conflicts with a ruling derived through theoretical qiyas or istislah, custom takes precedence. The author explains that Islamic ijtihads agree to leave the qiyasi ruling for custom, provided the custom does not contradict a specific or general text. Custom is considered evidence of need, making it stronger than qiyas. Ibn al-Humam equates the authority of custom to that of consensus in the absence of text. For Hanafis and Malikis, this preference is a form of istihsan. Finally, the author extends the principle to istislah (public interest rulings), since istislah lacks a textual basis and is more changeable than custom. Students should note that this rule applies only when no explicit text exists; custom cannot override a clear nass.

Key Terms
القياس النظري (al-qiyas al-nadhari): Theoretical analogy based on an inferred cause from a textual ruling, without direct text on the branch.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A method of juristic preference where a qiyas ruling is abandoned for a stronger evidence such as custom.
الاستصلاح (al-istislah): Consideration of public interest (maslaha) in the absence of text or qiyas.
العرف (al-urf): Customary practice of the people that is not contradictory to a direct text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason custom is considered stronger than theoretical qiyas?
A) Because custom is based on divine revelation.
B) Because custom is often evidence of need.
C) Because qiyas is always weak.
D) Because all Islamic schools agree on it.

Key Takeaway
Custom overrides theoretical qiyas and istislah when there is no direct textual ruling, because custom indicates need and has quasi-consensus authority in the absence of text.
